Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Preserving Classical Ash'ari Curriculum
Editor decries modern educational reforms and calls for return to authentic Ash'ari curriculum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's critique of modern educational reforms in Dagestan and his argument for preserving the classical Ash'ari curriculum.

Core Idea
Abandoning classical texts weakens students' creed and identity.

In this passage, the editor of Ithāf al-Murīd expresses deep concern over contemporary educational changes in Dagestan that replace the traditional Ash'ari curriculum with simplified modern works. He argues that this shift not only confuses students but also undermines their connection to their authentic Sunni heritage. The following text lays out his diagnosis and passionate call to return to the proven methodology of early scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with attention
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the editor's emotional tone and his specific criticisms of modern educational trends.
The passage begins by describing the current time as foggy and confused.
2
Identify the key problems
List the problems the editor identifies: weak summaries and memoranda replacing classical works, modern creed books with strange mixing, and curricula disconnected from Dagestan's authentic scientific methodology.
He gives examples from 'legal institutes' teaching contemporary creed books.
3
Understand the consequences
Note the editor's claim that these reforms produce students who are wavering, accept contradictions, and no longer see the necessity of adhering to the Ash'ari and Maturidi methodology.
He says such students become intellectually 'liquid' and consider adherence as bigotry.
4
Reflect on the solution
The editor calls for returning to the authentic heritage and classical curricula that Dagestan followed for centuries, emphasizing the role of early scholars in preserving the correct methodology.
He asks rhetorically how one can renounce the Sunni Ash'ari identity of these lands.
5
Summarize the main argument
Write a one-sentence summary of the editor's main point: the separation from authentic culture and destruction of heritage is the nation's greatest problem.
This is directly stated at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على الترقي في العلوم، خُصوصا في هذا الزمان الذي خيَّم عليه الضباب واختلط الحابل بالنابل ضاعت فيه المناهج التعليمة، حيث انفصلنا عن ثقافتنا وأصبحت كتب التراث الأصيل تأكُلُها أَرَضَةُ دعوى التجديد والتهذيب، تخليصاً مِن صعوبتها ،وتعقيدها مُدَّعين بهذا مُسايرة العصر، واستبدالها بأسلوب ميسر ، فظهرت المُلخصات والمُذَكِّرَات الضعيفة ، ولكم هو مُحزِنٌ أن تجد في مدارس ومعاهد داغستان مناهجاً وكُتبا في السلم التعليمي لا تمتُ بأي صلة إلى تاريخها ومَنْهَجِها العلمي الأصيل الذي ورثوه كابرًا عن كابر ، فتجد معاهدَ شرعية تُدَرِّسُ كتبا حديثة معاصرة في العقيدة - مثلا - فيها خلط عـ عجيب بحيث لا تبني طالب علم راسخ مُتَّبع لمنهج أهل السنة ، بل تُصيّره مُتذبذبا ذا ميوعة فكرية يقبل المُتناقضات ويستسيغها، ولا يرى ضرورةً في التّمسك بمنهج أهل السُّنَّة الأشاعرة والماتريدية ومن وافقهم، ويعتبر ذلك بجهله تعصباً وانغلاقًا، أو تجدهم يُدخِلُونَ في مناهجهم كُتبا ومتونا ليست منهجية ولا ألفت قبل استقرار المصطلحات ، ولا تناسب السلم التعليمي الذي وضعوه ، فيُضِيعُ الطالب ويَتَشتَّتُ أيما تَشتُتٍ ، وهذا أمرٌ قد عمَّ بلاؤه في الآونة الأخيرة. وفي أسواء الأحوال قد تجد أناسا يدرسون كتبا منهج أهل السنة الذي سارت عليه داغستان قرونا بتوفيق الله وبفضل جهود علمائها وأئمتها الكبار السابقين الذين لم يتركوا مجالا للمبتدعة ومناهجهم الفاسدة، فكيف يَحْلُوا لهم ذلك الآن ؟ وكيف يَسْتَسِيغُونَ التَنَكَّر لهويتهمُ السُّنيَّة الأشعرية في هذه الديار التي جُلُّ تاريخها الإسلامي وميراثها العلمي سني أشعري ؟! نسألُ الله أنْ يُصلح الأحوال ، مُشكلة أُمتنا الكبرى هي انفصالنا عن ثقافتنا الأصيلة بهدم التراث والمنهاج العلمية القديمة،
Upon advancement in the sciences, especially in this time in which fog has settled and confusion has spread, educational curricula have been lost. We have become separated from our culture, and the books of authentic heritage are being eaten by the moth of the claim of renewal and refinement, as an attempt to free from their difficulty and complexity, claiming thereby to keep pace with the times, replacing them with an easy style. Thus weak summaries and memoranda appeared. How sad it is to find in schools and institutes of Dagestan curricula and books in the educational ladder that have no connection to their history and authentic scientific methodology inherited from forefathers. You find legal institutes teaching modern contemporary books in creed, for example, containing strange mixing such that they do not build a firmly grounded student following the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna, but rather make him wavering with intellectual liquidity, accepting contradictions and relishing them, and he does not see the necessity of adhering to the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna al-Ash‘ariyya and al-Maturidiyya and those who agree with them, and considers that out of ignorance as bigotry and closure. Or you find them inserting into their curricula books and texts that are not methodologically sound, nor were authored before the stabilization of technical terms, nor suitable for the educational ladder they have set, so the student is lost and scattered greatly. This calamity has become widespread in recent times. In worst cases, you find people studying books that contradict the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna which Dagestan followed for centuries, by Allah's success and by the efforts of its great scholars and previous imams who left no room for innovators and their corrupt methodologies. So how can they now find that justified? And how can they tolerate renouncing their Sunni Ash‘ari identity in these lands whose entire Islamic history and scientific heritage is Sunni Ash‘ari? We ask Allah to rectify matters. The greatest problem of our nation is our separation from our authentic culture by destroying the heritage and the old scientific curricula.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a critique of modern educational reforms that have replaced classical texts with weak summaries and modern works in Dagestan. The editor argues that this leads to students becoming weak and wavering in their creed, losing the Ash‘ari heritage. He emphasizes the need to return to the traditional curriculum based on the works of the classical scholars. The passage serves as a call to preserve the Sunni Ash‘ari methodology.

Key Terms
التراث الأصيل
المناهج التعليمة
أهل السنة الأشاعرة
داغستان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sticking to classical texts is mere traditionalism and hinders progress.
Correction: The editor argues that abandoning classical texts leads to weak, wavering students who lose their creed and identity; classical texts are essential for building a firmly grounded student who follows the correct methodology.

Checkpoint
According to the editor, what is the major problem of the Muslim nation?
A) Lack of modern technology
B) Separation from authentic culture and destruction of heritage
C) Too many schools
D) Not enough teachers

Key Takeaway
The editor argues that abandoning classical Ash'ari curricula in favor of modern simplified texts leads to weak, wavering students who lose their authentic identity and creed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever chosen a simplified summary of a creedal text over the original because it was easier, and in doing so, did you lose the depth that would have built firm conviction in your heart?
?
Are there areas in your life where you have accepted contradictions in your beliefs because it was more convenient than holding firmly to the methodology of Ahl al-Sunna? What will you do about that today?
?
The editor laments that students no longer see adherence to Ash'ari methodology as necessary, thinking it is bigotry. Is there a teaching or practice you have abandoned because it felt outdated, even though it was part of the authentic heritage of your scholars? Will you return to it?

Editor's Methodology and Selection of Commentaries
The editor explains his methodology for selecting and including commentaries in this edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's specific methodology for selecting and integrating commentaries in this edition of the Jawharat al-Tawhid.

Core Idea
Understanding the editor's selective approach unlocks the structure of this textbook edition.

The editor, Sheikh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid al-Azhari, carefully chose which parts of famous commentaries to include. This lesson explains his two main reasons for not including the full commentaries and how his teaching experience shaped the selection.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's introduction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full context of the editor's explanation.
2
Identify the two reasons for selectivity
Look for the two key reasons the editor gives for not including the full commentaries of al-Amir and al-Shanwani.
3
Understand the selective criteria
Note how the editor used his teaching experience to pick comments from different sections of the book.
4
Recognize the editor's future plan
The editor mentions his intention to publish the full commentary of al-Amir separately—notice this point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المحقق
للشيخ محمد محي الدين عبد الحميد الأزهري له ، وبعد ثلاث سنوات من تدريسه تكونت لدي خُطة متكاملة للعمل على إخراجه بصورةٍ تَخْدِمُ طَلابَ العلم وتُسَهِّلُ عليهم تناوله بإذن الله ؛ فاستعنت بالله وتوكلت عليه في تحقيقه ووضع تعليقات وحواش عليه لأئمة كبار .
وفيما يلي عَرض سريع لتلك الحواشي والمنهج المتبع في إدراجها على الشرح:
حاشية العلامة شمس الدین محمد بن أحمد المالكي المالكي الأشعري المعروف بالأمير الكبير ه ، وهي حاشية جليلة القدر ، عظيمة النفع ، دقيقة العبارة ، وقد قالوا: كلام) الأمير أمير «الكلام» وتعتبر هذه الحاشية من أكبر إنتاجات العلامة الأمير الكلامية، ومن ميزاته أنه يصرح بالنقل عن حواشي من سبقه مِن شيوخه، كالعلامة الملوي والعدوي وغيرهما رحمهما الله جميعا، وينقل عنهم فوائد في حل الإشكالات تلقفها من مجالسهم ودروسهم المباركة، وقد يستدرك عليهم أحيانا.
ملاحظة : لم أدرج حاشية الأمير كاملة ولا حاشية العلامة الشنواني، بل اتبعتُ منهج الانتقاء لسببين رئيسين:
- ألا ألا يخرج الكتابُ في مجلدين، بل أحببت أن يخرج في مجلد عند تعليقه على قوله : (صفات ذاته في السطر ١٦ ، قال : وإنما كانت صفات المعنوية حادثة عند الأشاعرة والصواب كما لا يخفى صفات الأفعال.
ما يلي:
ومنها فى أوائل الحاشية على مسألة الوعد والوعيد في الصفحة: (١٤٤) ورد فذهب الأشاعرة إلى أنه يجوز أن يغفر الله الذنوب ويدخل مرتكبها النار» والصواب كما هو واضح ولا يدخل).
مقدمة المحقق
متوسط ، لكي يسهل على الطلاب شراؤه وحمله إلى الدروس. - ولأنَّ الكتابَ كِتابٌ دَرْسي ، فهو موجه لطلاب العلم الشّرعي . لمستوى مُحدَّد ومُعيَّن ، وانتقيتُ الحواشي حَسْب خبرتي المتواضعة في تدريس الكتاب ، فاتبعتُ طريقةً مُعيَّنة في اختيار الحواشي سرتُ عليها، حيث تركتُ غالب الحواشي المتعلقة بالأمور اللغوية والتوجيهات النحوية، لخروجها، ولا أدَّعي عدم فائدتها، بل هي نافعة جدا، ولكن المقام مقام اختصار ،وانتقاء، ولكي لا يتشتت ذهن الطالب ، ومع ذلك وضعتُ أغلب ما في الحاشيتين على قسم النُّبوات والسمعيات والتصوف، وهي وهي الأقسام التي خلت عن حاشية الشيخ محمد الدين فناسب المقام، وهو الإلهيات فلأن حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين قد كفَّتْ ووفَّتْ بأغلب أغلب حواشي الأمير والشنواني في هذا
محي
أما
القسم الأول
ما في الحواشي، كان يؤدي وضع القسم إلى تكرار كثير جدا، فاخترتُ تعليقات من الحاشيتين على بعض
المواضع التي لم تشملها حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين على هذا القسم. وأرجوا الله تعالى أن أكون قد وُفِّقْتُ ذلك (١) .
في
وحاشية العلامة محمد الشَّنواني الشافعي الأشعري ، شيخ الأزهر في زمانه ، ه ، وحاشيته تتميز بالاطلاع على جهود من سبقه مِن الأعلام، وخصوصا حاشية العلامة الأمير ، وتجد ذلك واضحا من خلال حاشيته،
من
(۱) ولأهمية حاشية العلامة الأمير الكبير هذه .. عزمت على تحقيقها وإخراجها كاملة في طبعة مستقلة تليق بها بإذن الله ، وقد تجمّع لدي بفضل الله تعالى ما يزيد على (٣٥) مخطوطا هذه الحاشية المباركة منها نسخ نفيسة عليها تعليقات وتقريرات كثيرة ، سأخرجها مع الحاشية بإذن الله تعالى، أسأل الله تعالى أن يوفقني لإتمامه وإخراجه في أقرب وقت ممكن .
Introduction of the editor: By Sheikh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid al-Azhari, after three years of teaching it, I developed a comprehensive plan to work on its production in a manner that serves students of knowledge and facilitates their access to it with God's permission. I sought help from God and relied on Him in its verification and placement of comments and footnotes upon it from great imams. Below is a quick presentation of those footnotes and the methodology followed in inserting them into the explanation: The footnote of the distinguished scholar Shams al-Din Muhammad ibn Ahmad al-Maliki al-Maliki al-Ash'ari known as al-Amir al-Kabir. It is a footnote of great stature, immense benefit, precise phrasing, and they said: 'The speech of al-Amir is the prince of speech.' This footnote is considered among the greatest scholarly productions of al-Amir in theological rhetoric. Among its features is that he explicitly narrates from the footnotes of those who preceded him from his teachers, such as al-Mallawi and al-'Adawi, may God have mercy on them all, and he transmits from them benefits in solving problematic issues that he gleaned from their gatherings and blessed lessons, and he sometimes critiques them. Note: I did not include the footnote of al-Amir in full nor the footnote of al-Shanwani in full; rather, I followed a method of selection for two main reasons: First, so that the book does not come out in two volumes; I preferred that it come out in one volume. In his commentary on his statement: 'The attributes of His essence in line 16', he said: 'And the attributes of meaning are considered originated according to the Ash'aris, and the correct view, as is not hidden, is that they are attributes of actions.' The following: Among them, at the beginning of the footnote on the issue of promise and threat on page (144), it came: 'The Ash'aris went to say that it is permissible for God to forgive sins and to admit the committer of them into the Fire, and the correct view, as is clear, is that he does not enter.' Introduction of the editor: Moderate, so that it becomes easy for students to purchase and carry to lessons. And because the book is a textbook, it is directed to students of religious knowledge, at a specific and defined level. I selected the footnotes according to my modest experience in teaching the book. I followed a specific method in choosing the footnotes that I adhered to: I left most of the footnotes related to linguistic matters and grammatical explanations, because they are outside the scope, and I do not claim they have no benefit; rather they are very beneficial, but the context is one of abbreviation and selection, and so that the student's mind is not distracted. Despite that, I placed most of what is in the two footnotes on the section of Prophecy, the Sam'iyyat, and Sufism, these being the sections that lacked the footnote of Sheikh Muhammad al-Din, so the context suited it. As for the first section, which is the Divine matters, because the footnote of Sheikh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din sufficed and covered most of the footnotes of al-Amir and al-Shanwani in this section, placing what is in the footnotes would lead to much repetition. So I selected comments from the two footnotes on some places that the footnote of Sheikh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din did not cover in this section. I ask God Almighty that I have been successful in that. (1) And the footnote of the distinguished scholar Muhammad al-Shanwani al-Shafi'i al-Ash'ari, the Shaykh of al-Azhar in his time. His footnote is distinguished by its familiarity with the efforts of those who preceded him from the eminent scholars, especially the footnote of al-Amir, and you find that clearly through his footnote. (1) For the importance of this footnote of al-Amir al-Kabir, I resolved to verify it and produce it complete in a separate edition befitting it, God willing. I have collected, by God's grace, more than 35 manuscripts of this blessed footnote, including precious copies with many comments and notes. I will publish it along with the footnote, God willing. I ask God Almighty to grant me success in completing and producing it as soon as possible.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is the editor's introduction to the book, explaining his methodology for selecting and including commentaries. It begins with the editor identifying himself and mentioning his plan after three years of teaching. He then provides a quick overview of the footnotes he used, starting with the commentary of al-Amir al-Kabir, describing its high status and features, notably its precise phrasing and the fact that al-Amir explicitly cites from earlier scholars and occasionally corrects them. The editor then states that he did not include the full commentaries of al-Amir and al-Shanwani but followed a selective approach for two main reasons. The first reason is to keep the book in a single moderate volume for ease of purchase and portability by students. The second reason is that the book is a textbook, so he selected comments based on his teaching experience. He left out most linguistic and grammatical notes to avoid distraction, but included most of the commentary on the sections of prophethood, sam'iyyat, and Sufism, as those sections lacked sufficient annotations from another commentary (of Muhammad Muhyi al-Din). For the section on divine matters, he found that the commentary of Muhammad Muhyi al-Din already covered most of the material, so he only added selected comments from al-Amir and al-Shanwani on points not covered. He also briefly describes the commentary of al-Shanwani as being well-informed of earlier works, especially al-Amir's. Finally, in a footnote, he announces his intention to publish the full commentary of al-Amir separately, mentioning that he has collected over 35 manuscripts. The passage serves as a practical guide to understanding the structure and content of the present edition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this edition includes the full text of the commentaries.
Correction: The editor explicitly states he selected only portions of the commentaries to keep the book concise and tailored for students.

Checkpoint
What two main reasons does the editor give for not including the full commentaries of al-Amir and al-Shanwani?
A) To keep the book in one moderate volume and because it is a textbook requiring selective use based on his teaching experience.
B) To avoid copyright issues and because the commentaries are too long.
C) Because the commentaries were not available and because students prefer shorter books.
D) To focus only on linguistic and grammatical notes.

Key Takeaway
The editor intentionally selected portions of commentaries to keep the book concise and tailored for students.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor carefully selected which footnotes to include based on the needs of students. When you study, do you choose your resources with the same intentionality, or do you just use whatever is easiest? What could you do to be more deliberate in your learning materials?
?
The editor omitted valuable linguistic notes to avoid distracting students. Are there distractions in your own study environment—habits, tools, or sources—that you should set aside for the sake of focus? What would it cost you to remove them?
?
The editor saw his role as serving students and facilitating their access to knowledge. In your own life, how are you using your knowledge to serve others? Is your learning just for yourself, or do you have a plan to pass it on? If you knew you only had three years to teach, what would you prioritize?

Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
Editor's Methodology and the Three Commentaries
Editor explains selection method and describes three commentaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's selection criteria for the commentaries and the distinctive features of the three main commentaries used in this edition.

Core Idea
Editor balances brevity and comprehensiveness by selectively including commentary.

This passage is the editor's own introduction to his work. He explains why he chose certain commentaries over others and describes the unique contributions of each. Understanding this methodology helps you appreciate the structure of the book and the scholarly tradition behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's introduction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the editor's overall goal: to produce a medium-sized textbook for students.
Notice the editor mentions he taught the book, so his choices come from experience.
2
Identify the selection criteria
The editor left out most linguistic commentaries for brevity, even though he acknowledges their value. Find where he states this.
3
Note the special treatment of certain sections
He included most material from the two commentaries on Prophethood, Samʿiyyāt, and Sufism because another commentary did not cover them. Identify these sections.
The sections are النبوات (Prophethood), السمعيات (Samʿiyyāt), and التصوف (Sufism).
4
Study the descriptions of the three commentaries
Read the descriptions of al-Amīr, al-Shanwānī, and Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn's commentaries. Note their distinguishing features.
Pay special attention to how al-Shanwānī expanded on al-Amīr and how Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn treated issues as a whole.
5
Reflect on the purpose of this introduction
Why does the editor provide all this detail? Consider how it helps the reader understand the edition's structure and scholarly value.
The introduction also announces a future separate edition of al-Amīr's commentary.

مقدمة المحقق
متوسط ، لكي يسهل على الطلاب شراؤه وحمله إلى الدروس. - ولأنَّ الكتابَ كِتابٌ دَرْسي ، فهو موجه لطلاب العلم الشّرعي . لمستوى مُحدَّد ومُعيَّن ، وانتقيتُ الحواشي حَسْب خبرتي المتواضعة في تدريس الكتاب ، فاتبعتُ طريقةً مُعيَّنة في اختيار الحواشي سرتُ عليها، حيث تركتُ غالب الحواشي المتعلقة بالأمور اللغوية والتوجيهات النحوية، لخروجها، ولا أدَّعي عدم فائدتها، بل هي نافعة جدا، ولكن المقام مقام اختصار ،وانتقاء، ولكي لا يتشتت ذهن الطالب ، ومع ذلك وضعتُ أغلب ما في الحاشيتين على قسم النُّبوات والسمعيات والتصوف، وهي وهي الأقسام التي خلت عن حاشية الشيخ محمد الدين فناسب المقام، وهو الإلهيات فلأن حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين قد كفَّتْ ووفَّتْ بأغلب أغلب حواشي الأمير والشنواني في هذا القسم الأول ما في الحواشي، كان يؤدي وضع القسم إلى تكرار كثير جدا، فاخترتُ تعليقات من الحاشيتين على بعض المواضع التي لم تشملها حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين على هذا القسم. وأرجوا الله تعالى أن أكون قد وُفِّقْتُ ذلك (١) .
في
وحاشية العلامة محمد الشَّنواني الشافعي الأشعري ، شيخ الأزهر في زمانه ، ه ، وحاشيته تتميز بالاطلاع على جهود من سبقه مِن الأعلام، وخصوصا حاشية العلامة الأمير ، وتجد ذلك واضحا من خلال حاشيته،
من
(۱) ولأهمية حاشية العلامة الأمير الكبير هذه .. عزمت على تحقيقها وإخراجها كاملة في طبعة مستقلة تليق بها بإذن الله ، وقد تجمّع لدي بفضل الله تعالى ما يزيد على (٣٥) مخطوطا هذه الحاشية المباركة منها نسخ نفيسة عليها تعليقات وتقريرات كثيرة ، سأخرجها مع الحاشية بإذن الله تعالى، أسأل الله تعالى أن يوفقني لإتمامه وإخراجه في أقرب وقت ممكن .
التي
مقدمة المحقق
6
وينقل عن العلامة الأمير بقوله : وقال بعضهم دون التصريح باسمه رحمهما الله تعالى، فتميزت حاشيته بالتوسع ، وتسهيل العبارة، وتوضيح المواضع قد تقصر الأفهام عن دركها ، وقد يتصرف في عبارة الأمير فيوسعها، أو يبين قيدا مهما ، وقد يترك عبارته كما هي، وله حواش وتعليقات كثيرة مستقلة على مواضع كثيرة لم يحش عليها العلامة الأمير ، ومن ميزات هذه الحاشية ضبط المواضع المُشكلة من نظم جوهرة التوحيد، وبالجملة حاشية العلامة الشنواني أوسع وأكبر حجماً ونفعاً.
حاشية العلامة محمد محي الدين عبد الحميد الأزهري ، وسمَّاها: «النّظامُ الفَريدُ بتحقيقِ جَوْهَرة التَّوحيد» وتتميز حاشيته بالتعليق شرحا وبيانا على المسألة كَكُل في سبيكة واحدة، ولا يعلق غالبا على الكلمات والعبارات الجزئية فقط كعادة أصحاب الحواش . مع طول نفس في البيان، وترتيب بالغ، وتصوير للمسألة ، وذكر جهاتها، فيضع لك بعبارته الرشيقة السهلة خلاصة كلام أصحاب الحواشي ، وأجوبتهم على الاعتراضات التي قد ترد على كلام الشارح، فمثلا عند مبحث صفة القدرة، يعلُّق بتعليق واحدٍ مطول في بداية المبحث ، يتكلم فيه عن هذه الصفة من جهاتها الثلاثة ، من جهة تعريفها ، ومن جهة دليلها ، ومن جهة تعلقات هذه الصفة ، التعريج على الخلافات الواقعة في الجهات الثلاثة إن وجدت ، هذه طريقته غالبا . وتجدر الإشارة إلى أن عمل الشيخ محمد محي الدين عبد الحميد لم يشمل كامل الشرح ، بل توقف مع نهاية قسم الإلهيات وبداية قسم النبوات ، وبالتحديد كانت آخر حاشية له عند شرح قول الناظم: وَمِنْهُ إِرْسَالُ جَمِيعِ الرُّسُلِ فَلَا وُجُوبَ بَلْ بِمَحْضِ الفَضْلِ
Investigator's Introduction: Medium-sized, so that it is easy for students to buy and carry to lessons. And because the book is a textbook, it is directed to students of legal knowledge at a specific and determined level. I selected the commentaries according to my modest experience in teaching the book, so I followed a specific method in choosing the commentaries that I proceeded upon, leaving out most of the commentaries related to linguistic matters and grammatical directions, because they are extraneous. I do not claim they are useless; rather they are very beneficial, but the situation is one of abbreviation and selection, and so that the student's mind is not scattered. Nevertheless, I included most of what is in the two commentaries on the section of Prophethood, Samʿiyyāt, and Sufism, and they are the sections which lacked the commentary of Shaykh Muḥammad al-Dīn, so it suited the situation. As for the divine attributes (Ilāhiyyāt), because the commentary of Shaykh Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn sufficed and fulfilled most of the commentaries of al-Amīr and al-Shanwānī in this first section. Including what is in the commentaries would have led to a great deal of repetition, so I selected comments from the two commentaries on some places not covered by the commentary of Shaykh Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn on this section. I ask Allah Almighty to have granted me success in that. (1) (1) And due to the importance of this great commentary of al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr, I resolved to edit and publish it completely in a separate edition worthy of it, Allah willing. Allah has blessed me with more than 35 manuscripts of this blessed commentary, including precious copies with many annotations and glosses. I will publish them with the commentary, Allah willing. I ask Allah to grant me completion and publication as soon as possible. The commentary of al-ʿAllāma Muḥammad al-Shanwānī al-Shāfiʿī al-Ashʿarī, Shaykh al-Azhar in his time, his commentary is distinguished by its awareness of the efforts of those who preceded him among the scholars, especially the commentary of al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr, and you find that clearly through his commentary. He transmits from al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr saying: 'And some of them said' without explicitly naming him – may Allah have mercy on them both. So his commentary is characterized by expansiveness, simplification of expression, clarification of places where understanding may fall short. He may alter the wording of al-Amīr, expanding it or clarifying an important condition, and sometimes leaves his wording as is. He has many independent commentaries and annotations on many places where al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr did not comment. Among the distinguishing features of this commentary is the correction of problematic passages in the poem Jawharat al-Tawḥīd. In short, the commentary of al-ʿAllāma al-Shanwānī is more extensive and greater in size and benefit. The commentary of al-ʿAllāma Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn ʿAbd al-Ḥamīd al-Azharī, which he named 'al-Niẓām al-Farīd bi-Taḥqīq Jawharat al-Tawḥīd'. His commentary is distinguished by commenting with explanation and clarification on the entire issue in a single strand, and he does not generally comment on individual words and phrases only, as is the habit of commentary writers. With lengthy exposition, excellent organization, depiction of the issue, and mention of its aspects. He presents for you in his elegant, easy wording the essence of the speech of the commentary writers and their answers to objections that may be raised against the explainer's words. For example, in the discussion of the attribute of power, he makes a single long comment at the beginning of the discussion, in which he speaks about this attribute from its three aspects: from the aspect of its definition, from the aspect of its proof, and from the aspect of the relations of this attribute, touching upon the differences occurring in these three aspects if any exist. This is his usual method. And it should be noted that the work of Shaykh Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn ʿAbd al-Ḥamīd did not cover the entire explanation, but rather stopped at the end of the section on divine attributes (Ilāhiyyāt) and the beginning of the section on Prophethood (Nubuwwāt). Specifically, his last commentary was at the explanation of the poet's saying: 'And from it [i.e., from the attribute of will] is the sending of all messengers, so there is no obligation, but rather by pure grace.'
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an editorial introduction explaining the method used in selecting and presenting the commentaries that accompany the main text (Jawharat al-Tawḥīd). The editor begins by stating his goal: to produce a medium-sized textbook for students, so the book must be concise and focused. He personally chose the commentaries based on his teaching experience. His selection method was to omit most commentaries that deal with linguistic or grammatical matters, not because they are useless, but to keep the student's mind from scattering and to stay within the goal of abbreviation. However, he made an exception for the sections on Prophethood, Samʿiyyāt (theological matters heard through revelation), and Sufism, because the commentary of Shaykh Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn did not cover those sections. So he included most of the material from the two commentaries (al-Amīr and al-Shanwānī) on those sections. For the section on divine attributes (Ilāhiyyāt), he found that Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn's commentary already sufficed and covered most of what the other two commentaries said, so including them would cause repetition. Therefore, he only added selected notes from the other two on points not covered by Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn. The editor then gives a detailed description of the three main commentaries he used. The first is the commentary of al-ʿAllāma al-Amīr, which the editor plans to publish separately because of its importance. The second is the commentary of al-ʿAllāma al-Shanwānī, who was a Shāfiʿī Ashʿarī scholar and Shaykh al-Azhar. His commentary is characterized by being well-informed about earlier works, especially al-Amīr's. Al-Shanwānī often quotes al-Amīr anonymously. His commentary is expansive, simplifies expression, clarifies difficult points, and sometimes modifies al-Amīr's wording by expanding or adding important conditions. He also provides independent commentaries on passages that al-Amīr did not cover. A key feature is his correction of problematic passages in the poem. In summary, al-Shanwānī's commentary is larger and more beneficial. The third commentary is by Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn ʿAbd al-Ḥamīd al-Azharī, titled 'al-Niẓām al-Farīd bi-Taḥqīq Jawharat al-Tawḥīd'. This commentary does not comment on isolated words or phrases but treats each theological issue as a whole (like a single strand). He provides a comprehensive explanation, including the issue's definition, proof, and aspects, along with responses to objections. For example, on the attribute of power, he gives one long comment covering its definition, its proof, and its relations, including any scholarly disagreements. The editor notes that this commentary is only partial: it covers the entire section on divine attributes but stops at the beginning of the section on prophethood, with the last comment on the line about the sending of messengers being an act of pure grace, not obligation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The editor omitted linguistic commentaries because he considers them unimportant.
Correction: He explicitly states they are very beneficial, but he omitted them for brevity and to keep the student focused.

Checkpoint
Why did the editor include most of the material from the two commentaries (al-Amīr and al-Shanwānī) on the sections of Prophethood, Samʿiyyāt, and Sufism?
A) Because those sections are more difficult.
B) Because the commentary of Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn did not cover those sections.
C) Because those sections are longer.
D) Because he preferred the two commentaries over Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn.

Key Takeaway
The editor's careful selection and detailed descriptions reveal the scholarly depth behind this edition and help the reader navigate the commentaries effectively.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor omitted useful linguistic commentaries for the sake of brevity and focus. What distractions in your own study of theology have you been unwilling to set aside? Are you willing to cut them out this week to gain deeper understanding?
?
Al-Shanwānī built upon al-Amīr's work without always naming him. Do you freely acknowledge the scholars from whom you learn, or do you subtly claim their insights as your own? How can you cultivate greater humility in your learning?
?
Muḥammad Muḥyī al-Dīn's commentary treats each issue as a whole, connecting definition, proof, and aspects. Do you tend to learn theology in fragmented pieces, missing the big picture? What is one topic you will study holistically this month, tying together its proofs and implications?

Method of Learning
beginner

Editor's Methodology and Humility
Editor explains his decision to supplement the commentary and his method.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's rationale for presenting supplementary glosses verbatim and his humble acknowledgment of scholarly limitations.

Core Idea
Good scholarship often reproduces faithfully what it cannot reforge.

This passage reveals the editor’s thoughtful approach to completing an unfinished commentary. Rather than claim to match the skill of his predecessor, he chooses to present the words of other scholars exactly as they wrote them. His humility and honesty about his own limitations are as instructive as the method itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the editor's tone—he is not boasting but explaining a practical decision.
Pay attention to the phrase 'أنَّى لي تلك المَلكة' which expresses his humility.
2
Identify the editorial dilemma
Compare the two methods: the predecessor's synthesis vs. the editor's verbatim reproduction. What problem does the editor face?
The predecessor covered only part of the book.
3
Reflect on the editor's humility
Note how the editor admits his inability to fuse the glosses into a smooth whole. Why is this acknowledgement significant for a scholar?
This prevents misattribution and respects the original authors.
4
Consider the closing prayers and colophon
The passage ends with prayers for the scholars and a detailed colophon. What does this add to the scholarly work?
It expresses gratitude and provides historical context.

ولأنَّ حاشية الشيخ محمد محي الدين غير شاملة لجميع أقسام الكتاب .. فهو ما حدا بي إلى فكرة اختيار حواش مُتمّمة على باقي أقسام الكتاب ، بحيثُ يجد الطالب بغيته بإذن الله ، وآثرتُ أن ألتزم وضع عبارات الحاشيتين كما هي دون تصرف في عبارات العلماء غالباً ، وإن لم تكن هذه الطريقة موافقة للطريقة التي جرى عليها الشيخ محمد محي الدين في حاشيته على القسم الأول ؛ من دمج الحواشي في عبارة يصوغها هو بطريقة سلسة وترتيب موفق ، وأنَّى لي تلك المَلكة والقدرة على صياغة الحواشي في سبيكة متماسكة . جزى الله هؤلاء الأئمة الأعلام عنا وعن المسلمين خير الجزاء، على ما بذلوه وتركوه لنا من هذا التراث والإرث النبوي العظيم، ونسأل الله أن يوفقنا لخدمة شريعته التي ارتضاها لأُمَّة خاتم الأنبياء سيدنا وقائدنا وقرة أعيننا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، والحمد لله رب العلمين. وكتبه محمد سيد بن يَحْيَى الدَّاغِسْتَانِيَ في جامعة الإمام أبي الحسن الأشعري الإسلامية ، في مدينة خاسافيورت / داغستان ليلة الخميس في التاسع والعشرين من شهر ربيع الأنوار شَهْرِ ظُهورِ الرَّحمة المُهداة صلَّى الله عليه وآله وسلَّم . ١٤٤١/٣/٢ هـ - الموافق لـ ٢٠١٩/٩/٩م
And because the gloss of Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din does not cover all sections of the book, this is what led me to the idea of selecting supplementary glosses on the remaining sections of the book, so that the student may find his goal, by the permission of Allah. And I preferred to adhere to putting the expressions of the two glosses as they are, without altering the words of the scholars generally, even though this method does not agree with the method followed by Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din in his gloss on the first section, of merging the glosses into a flowing expression and successful arrangement. And how could I have that ability and power to formulate the glosses into a coherent whole? May Allah reward these eminent imams on our behalf and on behalf of the Muslims with the best reward for what they have expended and left for us of this great prophetic heritage and legacy. And we ask Allah to grant us success in serving His Sharia which He has chosen for the nation of the Seal of the Prophets, our master, our leader, and the delight of our eyes, Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. And praise be to Allah, Lord of the worlds. Written by Muhammad Sayyid ibn Yahya al-Daghistani at Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari Islamic University, in the city of Khasavyurt, Dagestan, on the night of Thursday, the twenty-ninth of the month of Rabi' al-Anwar, the month of the appearance of the gifted mercy, may Allah bless him and his family and grant them peace. 2/3/1441 AH, corresponding to 9/9/2019 CE.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage explains the editor's rationale and methodology. Because Muhammad Muhyi al-Din's commentary is incomplete, the editor decided to select supplementary glosses from other scholars (specifically mentioned as 'the two glosses', likely al-Shanwani and possibly another) to cover the remaining parts. He chose to reproduce their words verbatim, without modification, unlike Muhammad Muhyi al-Din's method of synthesizing multiple glosses into a smooth, unified text. The editor acknowledges his inability to follow that superior method. He then prays for the scholars whose works he uses, and asks for divine assistance in serving the Sharia. The passage concludes with the editor's name, affiliation, location, and the date of writing.

Misconception: The editor thinks his method of verbatim reproduction is superior to his predecessor's synthesis.
Correction: The editor explicitly states his method is different and that he lacks the ability to imitate the predecessor's superior synthesis.

Why did the editor choose to present the supplementary glosses without modifying their wording?
A) He believed their wording was perfect
B) He lacked the ability to synthesize them like Muhammad Muhyi al-Din did
C) He wanted to preserve the original style of each scholar
D) He was in a hurry to complete the book

Key Takeaway
The editor humbly reproduces the glosses verbatim because he cannot match his predecessor's skill, showing that scholarly honesty is a virtue.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The editor states he prefers to keep the words of the scholars without alteration. In your own life, do you sometimes alter or distort what you learn to make it easier or more impressive? How can you commit to being faithful in transmitting knowledge as you received it?

Manuscript Analysis in Islamic Scholarship
Description of manuscripts used in the edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars use manuscript evidence to verify texts and identify discrepancies in copying dates.

Core Idea
Manuscripts carry hidden clues that reveal their true history.

This passage from the editor's introduction shows how scholars work with physical manuscripts to establish reliable texts. You'll see how a simple date can be questioned using internal evidence from the work itself. The editor does not accept the copyist's date at face value but corrects it based on when the commentary was authored.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the details given about each manuscript: condition, number of folios, copyist, and date.
2
Identify the editor's critique
The editor explicitly rejects the date given by the copyist of manuscript (A). Find the reason given in the text. What evidence does he use?
The year of authorship of the gloss is 1047 AH, which is later than 1011 AH.
3
Reflect on the methodology
Why is it important for an editor to be critical of manuscript dates? How does this affect our trust in the text?
This shows that texts are not blindly accepted; scholarly verification is key.

۱ - صور مخطوط المتن: منظومة جوهرة التوحيد المستعان بها في تصحيح وضبط المنظومة.
۲ - صور مخطوطات الشرح : إتحاف المريد» المستعان بها في تحقيق النص.
النسخة: (أ)
نسخة كاملة ، مقروءة ، ملونة المتن بالأحمر ، وعليها حواش وتقريرات وتصحيحات ، بها أثر بلل في أول ۱۰ لوحات ، وهي مع ذلك صالحة للقراءة، عدد أوراقها : ۷۱ ورقة ، وعدد الأسطر في الورقة الواحدة: (٢١). والناسخ: محمد بن محمد الريداني ، وقد ورد تأريخ سنة نسخها: (١٠١١ هـ) ونجزم بأن هذا التاريخ لا يصح ، حيث أنَّ سنة تأليف الحاشية كما سبق وأشرنا هي (١٠٤٧ هـ ) .
أرقام حفظ هذه النسخة في المكتبة الأزهرية : (٣٢٤٠ توحيد) ٤١٩٣٧ .
النسخة (ب)
نسخة كاملة حسنة الخط ملونة المتن باللون الأحمر، خالية عن الحواشي ،والتعليقات، عدد أوراقها : (٦٤) ورقة ، وعدد أسطرها في الورقة
Manuscripts relied upon in the work:
1 - Photographs of the text manuscript: 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' poem used in correcting and fixing the poem.
2 - Photographs of the commentary manuscripts: 'Ithaf al-Mureed' used in verifying the text.
Copy (A):
A complete, readable copy, with the text colored in red, and it has marginal notes, glosses, and corrections. It has traces of dampness in the first 10 folios, but it is still readable. Number of folios: 71, number of lines per folio: 21. The copyist: Muhammad ibn Muhammad al-Raydani. The date of copying is recorded as 1011 AH, but we assert this date is incorrect, since the year of authorship of the gloss is, as we previously mentioned, 1047 AH.
Archive numbers of this copy in the Azhar Library: (3240 Tawhid) 41937.

A complete, well-written copy, with the text colored in red, free of marginal notes and comments. Number of folios: 64, number of lines per folio: [cut off].

Commentary
This passage describes the manuscripts used in the edition. It lists two types: the text manuscript (Jawharat al-Tawhid) and the commentary manuscripts (Ithaf al-Mureed). Copy (B) is briefly described but cuts off at the number of lines. The passage illustrates the editor's methodology in relying on physical manuscripts and his awareness of dating issues.

Correction: The editor shows that dates must be checked against internal evidence, such as the composition date of the work. The gloss was written in 1047 AH, so a copy dated 1011 AH is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the editor reject the copying date of manuscript (A)?
A) Because it is too old
B) Because it conflicts with the composition date of the gloss
C) Because the copyist was unreliable
D) Because the ink was faded

Key Takeaway
Editors of classical texts must critically evaluate manuscript evidence, including dates, to ensure accuracy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This editor refused to accept a date just because it was written down. What beliefs or teachings do you hold that you have never questioned? Do you have the courage to examine them as critically as this editor examined the manuscript?
?
The editor used internal consistency to uncover an error. Is there an inconsistency in your own understanding of Islamic doctrine that you have been ignoring? What evidence would convince you that you might be wrong?
?
If you discovered that a source you previously trusted contained a significant error, would you be willing to change your position? How attached are you to the opinions you hold, and what would it take to revise them?

Need for Abbreviation in Kalam
The poet states the necessity of knowing principles of religion and the reason for abbreviation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's rationale for writing an abbreviated work on the principles of religion.

Core Idea
Abbreviation in kalam is a response to practical need, not lack of depth.

The poet begins by affirming the necessity of knowing the principles of religion (usul al-din). He then explains that while such knowledge requires explanation, the extended length of traditional works has exhausted readers, making an abbreviated presentation a practical necessity. This couplet sets the stage for the concise work that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic couplet and translation
Read the Arabic couplet and its literal translation carefully. Appreciate the poetic rhythm and identify the key terms: أصل الدين, تطويل, اختصار.
This couplet is a complete methodological statement.
2
Identify the two parts
The couplet has two main parts: first, the obligation of knowing the principles of religion and its need for clarification; second, the problem of lengthy works causing weariness and the solution of abbreviation.
The conjunction 'لكن' (but) marks the shift.
3
Reflect on the pedagogical principle
Consider why conciseness can be beneficial without sacrificing essential content. The poet is not dismissing depth but responding to human limitations.
This reflects a common challenge in Islamic education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَبَعْدُ فَالْعِلْمُ بِأَصْلِ الدِّينِ مُحَتَّمٌ يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّبْيِينِ لَكِنْ مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ كَلَّتِ الْهِمَمُ فَصَارَ فِيهِ الِاخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمٌ

Commentary
This passage explains the need for this work. The author says that knowledge of the principles of religion (usul al-din) is obligatory and requires explanation. However, because lengthy works have caused people's resolve to weaken, it has become necessary to produce an abbreviated version.

Key Terms

Misconception: Abbreviation implies the work is shallow or omits essential details.

?

The Six Necessities Protected by Sacred Law
Enumeration of six obligatory preservations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six fundamental necessities that Islamic law aims to preserve and their significance in Maqasid al-Shari'ah.

Core Idea
Sacred law protects six essentials: religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor.

This brief line from the JaWharah al-Tawhid captures the heart of Islamic jurisprudence's purpose: the preservation of six fundamental human necessities. Understanding these six helps us see the wisdom behind legal rulings and the goals of the Shari'ah. Let's examine what they are and why their preservation is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic line once and then the translation. Notice the six items listed and the use of 'ثُمَّ' (then) and 'مثلها' (and like them).
The order indicates priority: religion first, then life, etc.
2
Identify the six necessities
List them in order: religion (دِين), life (نَفْس), property (مال), lineage (نَسَب), intellect (عَقْل), and honor (عِرْض). Note that honor is explicitly included alongside the more commonly known five.
Some classical lists have only five; this text adds honor as a sixth.
3
Understand why preservation is obligatory
Reflect on how each necessity is essential for human well-being and social order. The text says 'قد وجب' (has been obligatory), meaning the Shari'ah commands their protection.
This is a core principle of Maqasid al-Shari'ah (higher objectives of law).
4
Connect to broader Islamic teachings
Think of examples where Islamic law safeguards each necessity: e.g., penalties for apostasy protect religion, laws against murder protect life, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَحِفْظُ دِينِ ثُمَّ نفسٍ مال نسب ومثلُهَا عَقْلٌ وَعِرْضُ قد وجب
And preservation of religion, then life, property, lineage, and like them intellect and honor has been obligatory.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This line mentions the well-known five necessities (al-daruriyyat al-khams) that Islamic law aims to protect. However, here six are listed: religion (din), life (nafs), property (mal), lineage (nasab), intellect (aql), and honor (ird). The text states that their preservation is obligatory (wajib). This forms a foundational principle in Maqasid al-Shari'ah.

Key Terms

مال (property)
نسب (lineage)

Misconception: Some think only five necessities exist (religion, life, property, lineage, intellect) and honor is not a separate category.
Correction: The text explicitly includes honor (عِرْض) as the sixth necessity, showing its co- equal importance in Islamic law.

Checkpoint

A) Five
B) Six
C) Seven
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The preservation of these six necessities is a fundamental objective of Islamic law, showing that the Shari'ah aims to protect both spiritual and worldly well-being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Enjoining Good and Avoiding Vice
The passage commands good character and lists specific vices to avoid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific vices prohibited in this passage and the virtue of forbearance and truthfulness.

Core Idea
Good character requires avoiding slander, backbiting, and blameworthy traits.

The text begins by commanding what is known to be good and immediately lists several vices to avoid. These are not just general sins but specific blameworthy traits that damage social harmony. The passage also sets the positive example of the Prophet and the righteous as allies of forbearance and followers of truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the command
Notice the imperative verb 'wa'mur' (and enjoin) and the object 'bi-urf' (with what is good). This sets the tone for the entire passage.
2
List the prohibited vices
Read the names of the vices: namima (slander), ghiba (backbiting), ujb (vanity), kibr (pride), hasad (envy), mira' (disputation), jadal (argument). Notice the rhyme and rhythm.
3
Reflect on the positive ideal
The passage then says 'be as the best of creation were: an ally of forbearance, a follower of truth.' Consider how this contrasts with the vices.
The best creation refers primarily to the Prophet and the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأْمُرْ بعرف واجتنب نميمة وغِـيـبَـةٌ وَخَصْـلَةٌ ذَمِيـمَـة كالعُجْبِ والكِبْرِ وداءِ الحَسَدِ وَكَالمِرَاءِ والجَدَلْ فَاعْتَمِدِ وكُنْ كَمَا كَانَ خيارُ الخلقِ حَلِيفَ حِلْمٍ تابعا للحقِّ فَكُلُّ خيرٍ في اتِّبَاعِ من سَلَفْ وكلُّ شَرٌّ فِي ابْتِدَاعِ مَنْ خَلَفْ وكلُّ هدي للنَّبِيِّ قَدْ رَجَحْ فما أبيحَ افْعَلْ وَدَعْ ما لم يُبَحْ فتابعِ الصَّالِح ممَّنْ سَلَفا وجانب البدعة ممَّنْ خَلَفا
And enjoin what is known (as good) and avoid slander, and backbiting, and blameworthy trait, like vanity, pride, and the disease of envy, and like arguments and dispute, so rely. And be as the best of creation were: an ally of forbearance, a follower of truth. For all good is in following those who have passed, and all evil is in innovation of those who came later. And every guidance of the Prophet is preponderant, so do what is permitted and leave what is not permitted. So follow the righteous among those who have passed and avoid innovation among those who came later.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ethical exhortation. It begins by commanding what is known to be good (uruf) and prohibiting various vices: slander (namima), backbiting (ghiba), and other blameworthy traits such as vanity (ujb), pride (kibr), envy (hasad), disputation (mira'), and argumentation (jadal). The reader is told to rely on this. Then it instructs to be like the best of creation (the Prophet and his Companions) – an ally of forbearance (halif hilmin) and a follower of truth (tabi'an lil-haqq). The central principle is that all good lies in following the Salaf (predecessors) and all evil lies in the innovations (ibtida') of the later generations. The guidance of the Prophet is paramount; one should do what is permitted and leave what is not. Finally, it explicitly commands following the righteous Salaf and avoiding innovation from those who came after.

الحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage only commands general goodness without specifics.
Correction: It lists specific vices and traits, so it is very detailed.

Checkpoint

A) Slander
B) Backbiting
C) Lying
D) Vanity

Key Takeaway
This passage provides a clear list of vices to avoid and encourages cultivating forbearance and truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the vices listed in this passage—slander, backbiting, vanity, pride, envy, argumentation, or dispute—do you find yourself struggling with most? Why does this vice keep a grip on your heart? Write down one specific, measurable action you will take this week to uproot it, and ask a trusted friend to hold you accountable.
?
The passage commands you to enjoin good before listing what to avoid. When was the last time you intentionally advised someone towards good or defended the truth? What held you back—fear, laziness, or pride? Identify a relationship or situation where you have remained silent, and commit to speaking one word of truth or encouragement to that person tomorrow.
?
The best of creation were 'allies of forbearance' and 'followers of truth.' Honestly examine a recent conflict: did your reaction reflect forbearance or anger? What truth did you fail to follow because your ego wanted to 'win'? Right now, can you muster the humility to text or speak to that person and reconcile, even if you were in the right? If not, what stops you?

All Good in Following the Salaf
The passage establishes the principle that good is in following the Salaf and evil in innovation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that all good is in following the righteous predecessors and all evil is in religious innovation, and the command to follow the Salaf.

Core Idea
Following the Salaf brings good; innovation in religion brings evil.

The second part of the passage establishes a foundational principle of Sunni theology: that all goodness is found in adhering to the way of the Salaf (the righteous early Muslims), and all evil stems from religious innovations (bid'ah) introduced by later generations. It then instructs to follow the righteous Salaf and avoid innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle
The text states 'all good is in following those who have passed, and all evil is in innovation of those who came later.' Understand the contrast.
2
Understand 'following' and 'innovation'
Following (ittiba') means emulating the Salaf in beliefs and practices. Innovation (ibtida') refers to introducing new religious practices or beliefs without basis in Revelation.
3
Note the final command
The passage ends with a direct command: 'follow the righteous among those who have passed and avoid innovation among those who came later.'
4
Connect to the earlier ethical commands
Reflect on how this principle relates to the earlier emphasis on character traits—good character is part of the Salaf's way.

Who Are the People of the Sunnah? Defining Ahl al-Sunnah
Commentary defining Ahl al-Sunnah and explaining the terms in the author's opening line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Ahl al-Sunnah as including both Ash'aris and Maturidis, and the meaning of Sunnah as the Prophet's way embodied in the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Ahl al-Sunnah are those who follow the Prophet’s way, combining Qur’an and Hadith.

The commentator begins by defining the key term 'Ahl al-Sunnah' and clarifies its scope. This definition is crucial because it sets the boundaries of orthodox Islam and distinguishes it from deviant sects. The commentator also explains that the Sunnah is not separate from the Qur'an but is its living embodiment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of Ahl al-Sunnah
Read the passage and find the core definition of who are considered Ahl al-Sunnah. Notice that it includes those who follow the Prophet without innovation.
2
Understand the scope: Ash'aris and Maturidis
The commentator explicitly states that Ahl al-Sunnah includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis. Note the reasoning and the exclusion of deviant sects.
This clarifies a common misconception that the term excludes one of these groups.
3
Examine the refutation of a misconception
The commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding that 'Sunnah' is opposed to 'Kitab' (Qur'an). Understand why the name 'Ahl al-Sunnah' was chosen and how it differs from 'People of the Book'.
This reinforces that the Prophet's way is the lived interpretation of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مقدمة المصنف
اس العالم
الحمد لله الذي رفع لأهل السُّنَّة (۱) المحمدية في الخَافِقَين أَعلاما (۲) .
،
(۱) قوله: (الأهل السنة) أي: أصحاب طريقة النبي الله ، وهم الذين اتبعوه ولم يُحدثوا ما يخالفه ، وخرجت فرق الضلال، والمراد بأهل السنة : ما يشمل الأشاعرة والماتريدية ؛ وإن كانت المقدمة موضوعة على مُختار الأولى، وفي ذكره لأهل السنة في المقدمة براعة استهلال، والسنة: طريقة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وكان كما في الحديث [صحيح مسلم ٧٤٦] «خُلقه القرآن» وهي التي كان عليها السلف الصالح استندت لكتاب أو حديث ؛ فليس المراد بها ما قابل الكتاب حتى يُحتاج لما نقله شيخنا العدوي عن المؤلف في حاشيته مِن أنهم سموا أهل سنةٍ ولم يسموا أهل كتاب مع استنادهم لكل لإيهام اليهود والنصارى ؛ فإنهم اشتهروا بأهل الكتاب.
الأمير والشنواني. (۲) وصفَ الشَّارِحُ المحمود بالموصول وصلته ؛ لأنه يريد الحمد الذي يقع من الحامد في مقابلة نعمة وصلت إليه من المحمود ؛ لما قيل: إنَّ الحمد المُقيَّد خير من الحمد المطلق، ووجه ذلك : أنَّ الحامد حمدًا مقيدًا يُثاب على حمده هذا ثوابَ مَن فعل واجبًا، والحامد حمدا مطلقا يثاب ثوابَ مَن فعل مندوبا ، وإِنَّما كان ذلك كذلك ؛ لأنَّ الحمد المقيَّد شكر للمُنعم على نعمته ، وشكر المنعم واجب اتفاقا ، غير أن المعتزلة يقولون: أوجبه العقل. وهذا بناءً على ما ذهبوا إليه من التحسين والتقبيح العقليين ، وأهل السُّنَّة والجماعة يقولون: أوجبه
الشرع .
والمراد بالسُّنَّة: طريقة سيد التقلين سيدنا محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وطريقته: كلُّ ما جاء به القرآن الكريم ؛ لما ورد في الحديث من أنه كان : كان خُلقه القرآن . والخافقين في الأصل: المشرق والمغرب، والمراد بهما عموم الجهات، وتسمية هاتين الجهتين خافقين؛ لأنَّ الرَّياح تخفق فيهما: تضطرب ذاهبة وجائية، فهو مجاز عقلي بإسناد الفعل إلى مكان حصوله.
والأعلام : جمع عَلَم - بفتح العين المهملة واللام جميعاً ـ وهو في هذه الفقرة بمعنى الراية ، وقد جرت العادة بألا تُنصبَ الرَّايَاتُ إِلَّا فوق رؤوس عُظماء الناس؛ فهذه الفقرة كناية عن ثبوت صفة الرّفعة وعِظم الشَّأن لأهل السنة المحمدية. محي.
۹۳
Introduction of the author. [As?] the world. Praise be to Allah who raised for the Sunnah (1) Muhammadan in the two horizons banners (2). (1) His saying: (Ahl al-Sunnah) meaning: the companions of the way of the Prophet of Allah, and they are those who followed him and did not innovate what contradicts him, and the deviant sects are excluded, and what is meant by Ahl al-Sunnah: that which includes the Ash'aris and the Maturidis; even if the introduction is placed according to the preferred opinion, and in his mentioning of Ahl al-Sunnah in the introduction there is a good beginning, and Sunnah: the way of Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and he was, as in the hadith [Sahih Muslim 746], 'His character was the Qur'an', and it is that which the righteous predecessors were upon, relying on a book or a hadith; so what is meant is not that which contrasts with the book, such that it would need what our shaykh al-'Adawi transmitted from the author in his gloss that they called themselves people of Sunnah and did not call themselves people of the Book despite their reliance on each to mislead Jews and Christians; for they are famous as people of the Book. Al-Amir and al-Shanwani. (2) The commentator described the praised one by the relative clause and its connection; because he intends the praise that occurs from the praiser in return for a favor that reached him from the praised one; because it was said: indeed, restricted praise is better than absolute praise, and the reason for that is that the one who praises with restricted praise is rewarded for his praise with the reward of one who performed an obligation, and the one who praises with absolute praise is rewarded with the reward of one who performed a recommended act; and that is only thus because restricted praise is thanks to the Benefactor for His favor, and thanking the Benefactor is obligatory by agreement, except that the Mu'tazila say: reason obligated it. And this is based on what they held of rational subjective good and evil, and Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: the Law obligated it. And what is meant by Sunnah: the way of the master of the two worlds, our master Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and his way: everything that the Noble Qur'an came with; because of what is reported in the hadith that he was: 'His character was the Qur'an'. And 'al-khafiqqyn' originally: the east and the west, and what is meant by them is all directions, and the naming of these two directions as 'khafiqqyn' because the winds beat (takhfiq) in them: they fluctuate coming and going, so it is a mental metaphor by attributing the action to the place of its occurrence. And 'al-a'lam' is plural of 'alam - with the fatha of the unpointed 'ayn and lam both - and it is in this passage in the meaning of banner, and the custom is that banners are not erected except over the heads of great people; so this passage is a metaphor for the establishment of the attribute of elevation and great importance for the Muhammadan Ahl al-Sunnah. Muhi. 93.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the opening of the author's introduction. The commentator begins by defining 'Ahl al-Sunnah' (the People of the Sunnah). He explains that they are those who follow the Prophet's way and do not innovate against it, excluding deviant sects. Importantly, he clarifies that 'Ahl al-Sunnah' includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis, even though the introduction follows the preferred opinion of one school. He notes that mentioning Ahl al-Sunnah in the introduction is a 'bra'at al-istihlal' (a good opening). He then defines 'Sunnah' as the way of the Prophet, which was characterized by the Qur'an, as per the hadith. He refutes a potential misunderstanding that 'Sunnah' is opposed to 'Book' (Qur'an), explaining that the righteous predecessors relied on both, and the name 'Ahl al-Sunnah' was chosen to avoid the association with 'People of the Book' used by Jews and Christians. In the second part, the commentator discusses the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah'. He explains that the author used a relative clause to describe Allah because he intends praise that is a response to a specific favor. He argues that 'muqayyad' (restricted) praise is better than 'mutlaq' (absolute) praise because the former is an obligation (reward for wajib) while the latter is recommended (reward for mandub). This is because restricted praise is thanks (shukr) for a specific favor, and thanking the Benefactor is obligatory by consensus. He notes the Mu'tazila claim reason obligates it based on rational good and evil, while Ahl al-Sunnah say the Law obligates it. He then explains 'al-khafiqqyn' metaphorically as all directions, and 'al-a'lam' as banners, indicating the elevated status of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Key Terms
أهل السنة
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية
السنة
الخافقين
الأعلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ahl al-Sunnah refers only to those who follow the literal texts of the Qur'an and Hadith without including theological schools like Ash'aris and Maturidis.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that Ahl al-Sunnah includes both Ash'aris and Maturidis, and that they rely on both Qur'an and Hadith.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, which groups are included under Ahl al-Sunnah?
A) Only the Ash'aris
B) Only the Maturidis
C) Both Ash'aris and Maturidis
D) All Muslims except the Mu'tazila

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunnah are the followers of the Prophet's way, including Ash'aris and Maturidis, and their Sunnah is the embodied Qur'an.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator explains that the name 'Ahl al-Sunnah' was chosen to avoid the label 'People of the Book'. Are you more attached to the name of your group or to the substance of following the Prophet? What label do you need to set aside to be a true follower?
?
When you hear the banners of Ahl al-Sunnah being raised, does it ignite a longing to be among them, or does it make you complacent? What will you do this week to earn a place under those banners?

Restricted Praise vs. Absolute Praise: The Nature of Hamd
Commentary explaining why the author used a restricted praise and the superiority of restricted praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why restricted praise (al-hamd al-muqayyad) is superior to absolute praise and the theological debate on the basis of thanking the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Restricted praise is thanking for a specific favor, an obligation with greater reward.

The commentator next turns to the phrase 'al-hamdu lillah' in the author's introduction. He explains why the author used a restricted praise and why it is considered better than absolute praise. This discussion touches on the obligation of thanking Allah and the difference between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah on the source of that obligation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between restricted and absolute praise
Read the passage to see how the commentator describes the two types of praise: restricted (in response to a specific favor) and absolute (general praise).
2
Grasp the superiority of restricted praise
The commentator argues that restricted praise yields a reward equal to an obligatory act, while absolute praise yields reward of a recommended act. Understand the reasoning: restricted praise is shukr (thanks) which is wajib.
This is based on the consensus that thanking the Benefactor is obligatory.
3
Compare the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah views on obligation
The Mu'tazila claim reason obligates shukr, while Ahl al-Sunnah say the Law (shar') obligates it. Note the basis in rational good and evil (tahsin wa taqbih 'aqliyyayn).
This highlights a key theological difference concerning the source of moral obligation.

This passage is a commentary on the opening of the author's introduction. The commentator begins by defining 'Ahl al-Sunnah' (the People of the Sunnah). Restricted Praise vs Absolute Praise and the Obligation of Thankfulness
Commentator distinguishes muqayyad and mutlaq hamd and their rewards.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between restricted praise (al-hamd al-muqayyad) and absolute praise (al-hamd al-mutlaq), and the theological dispute over the basis of the obligation to thank the Benefactor.

Core Idea
Restricted praise is thankfulness, and thankfulness is obligatory—by reason or by Law?

This passage from the commentator clarifies why the author described God with a relative clause, leading to a comparison between two types of praise. The commentator also addresses a key theological question: what makes thankfulness to the Benefactor obligatory? The answers of the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah reveal a fundamental difference in their approaches to moral obligation.

Guided Path
1
Context of the relative clause
Understand why the commentator mentions the relative clause: to specify praise that is a response to a specific blessing.
2
Compare the two types of praise
Identify the difference between restricted (muqayyad) and absolute (mutlaq) praise and their respective rewards (obligatory vs recommended).
Notice that restricted praise is considered better.
3
Connect restricted praise to thankfulness
Realize that restricted praise is essentially thankfulness (shukr) for a blessing, and that thankfulness is obligatory by agreement.
4
Identify the dispute
Recognize the disagreement between the Mu'tazila (reason) and Ahl al-Sunnah (Law) over what necessitates this obligation.
This shows a key methodological difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصفَ الشَّارِحُ المحمود بالموصول وصلته؛ لأنه يريد الحمد الذي يقع من الحامد في مقابلة نعمة وصلت إليه من المحمود؛ لما قيل: إنَّ الحمد المُقيَّد خير من الحمد المطلق، ووجه ذلك: أنَّ الحامد حمدًا مقيدًا يُثاب على حمده هذا ثوابَ مَن فعل واجبًا، والحامد حمدا مطلقا يثاب ثوابَ مَن فعل مندوبا، وإِنَّما كان ذلك كذلك؛ لأنَّ الحمد المقيَّد شكر للمُنعم على نعمته، وشكر المنعم واجب اتفاقا، غير أن المعتزلة يقولون: أوجبه العقل. وهذا بناءً على ما ذهبوا إليه من التحسين والتقبيح العقليين، وأهل السُّنَّة والجماعة يقولون: أوجبه الشرع .
The commentator described the Praised One with the relative clause and its complement; because he intends the praise that occurs from the praiser in exchange for a blessing that reached him from the Praised One; because it was said: Indeed, restricted praise (al-hamd al-muqayyad) is better than absolute praise (al-hamd al-mutlaq). And the reason for that is that the one who offers restricted praise is rewarded for his praise with the reward of one who performed an obligatory act, and the one who offers absolute praise is rewarded with the reward of one who performed a recommended act. And that is only so because restricted praise is thankfulness to the Benefactor for His blessing, and thankfulness to the Benefactor is obligatory by agreement, except that the Mu'tazila say: reason necessitates it. And this is based on what they hold of rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan). And Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah say: the Law (al-shar') necessitates it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between two types of praise (hamd) and the theological implications. The commentator begins by noting that the author described God with a relative clause to indicate praise that is a response to a specific blessing, leading to a comparison between restricted praise (al-hamd al-muqayyad) and absolute praise (al-hamd al-mutlaq). It is stated that restricted praise is superior because the one who offers it is rewarded as for an obligatory act, while the one who offers absolute praise is rewarded as for a recommended act. The reason is that restricted praise constitutes thankfulness (shukr) to the Benefactor for His blessing. The commentator then states that thankfulness to the Benefactor is obligatory by agreement (ittifaqan). However, there is a dispute over the source of this obligation: the Mu'tazila hold that reason ('aql) necessitates it, based on their doctrine of rational good and evil (al-tahsin wa al-taqbih al-'aqliyyan). In contrast, Ahl al-Sunnah hold that the Law (shar') necessitates it.

Key Terms
الحمد المقيَّد
الحمد المطلق
الشكر
التحسين والتقبيح العقليين
المعتزلة
أهل السنة والجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation to thank the Benefactor is purely rational and agreed upon by all.
Correction: While all agree thankfulness is obligatory, Mu'tazila argue reason necessitates it, while Ahl al-Sunnah argue the Law necessitates it.

Checkpoint
What is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the obligation of thankfulness to the Benefactor?
A) Reason necessitates it
B) The Law necessitates it
C) It is not obligatory
D) It is recommended but not obligatory

Key Takeaway
The passage establishes that restricted praise, as thankfulness for a blessing, is rewarded as an obligatory act, and that the basis of this obligation is a point of theological dispute between reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah, do you mostly offer restricted praise (thanking Him for specific blessings) or absolute praise (praising Him without a particular cause)? Which type do you find easier, and what does that reveal about your attentiveness to His favors?
?
The passage says thankfulness to the Benefactor is obligatory, and that restricted praise carries the reward of an obligatory act. Do you treat your daily thankfulness with the same seriousness as an obligatory prayer? If not, what needs to change in your heart and habits?
?
Both Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunnah agree that thankfulness is obligatory, only differing on its source. Regardless of the theological debate, do you truly feel the weight of that obligation? Ask yourself: how often do I intentionally thank Allah for a specific blessing, and when I fail, do I feel I have neglected a duty?

The Author's Opening Praise and Testimony
Al-Laqani opens his commentary with praise of God, testimony, and blessings on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and significance of the author's opening doxology, including the three distinct declarations and their theological import.

Core Idea
The opening words establish the entire work as an act of devotion and testimony.

Al-Laqani begins his commentary with a traditional doxology that simultaneously praises Allah, declares His oneness, and blesses the Prophet. This opening is not merely decorative; it sets the tone for the entire work and grounds theological inquiry in faith and gratitude. As you read, notice the layered meanings of key terms like aʿlām.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the rhythm and flow of the doxology sink in before interpreting.
2
Identify the three components
The passage has three main parts: the praise of God for establishing proofs, the testimony of faith (shahada), and the blessings on the Prophet. Find each part.
3
Spot the homonymy of 'aʿlām'
The word 'aʿlām' appears three times with different meanings. Try to determine the meaning in each instance from the context.
This is a key rhetorical feature of the passage.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Think about why the author begins with these declarations before any theological arguments. What does this tell us about the nature of the work?
Consider the relationship between devotion and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووضع بواضح أدلتهم مِن شبه المخالفين أعلاما (1) ، وأشهد أن لا إله إلَّا الله (١) وحده لا شريك له ، شهادةً تكون بالتخلص في الدارين إعلامًا (۲) ، وأشهد أنَّ سيّدنا محمداً عبده ورسوله الممنوح من اتبعه مِن الجِنَانِ أَعلاما (۳) صلَّى الله وسلّم عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه ما أيّدت قواعد العقائد (٤) ، وما حُلِّيت الجياد بجواهر الفرائد (۱) .
And he placed as clear signs (aʿlām) their proofs from the doubts of the opponents. And I bear witness that there is no god but Allah alone, with no partner, a testimony that will be a means of deliverance in the two abodes, as an announcement. And I bear witness that our master Muḥammad is His servant and messenger, granted to those who follow him from the gardens (al-jinān) as lofty ranks (aʿlām). May Allah bless him and grant him peace, and upon his family and companions, as long as the foundations of the creeds are established, and as long as the necks are adorned with the jewels of unique pearls.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is the author's opening salutation and testimony. It is a traditional doxology that combines praise of God and witness to His oneness, followed by blessings on the Prophet. The author uses elevated language with multiple rhetorical figures (as indicated in the footnotes, but the main text itself employs phrases like 'aʿlām' and 'jinān'). The meaning: God has clearly established the proofs of the Sunnis against the doubts of their opponents. The testimony of faith is described as a means of salvation in this world and the next. The Prophet is described as one from whom his followers receive high ranks in Paradise. The blessings on the Prophet and his companions are linked to the enduring support of the foundations of faith (qawāʿid al-ʿaqāʾid) and the adornment of believers with the jewels of unique pearls (jawāhir al-farāʾid), i.e., the precious truths of theology.

Key Terms
أعلام
شبه
إعلام
الجنّان
قواعد العقائد
جواهر الفرائد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'aʿlām' has only one meaning in this passage.
Correction: In fact, 'aʿlām' is used in three distinct senses: (1) signs/landmarks (from the doubts of opponents), (2) an announcement (of the testimony), and (3) lofty ranks (in Paradise). This homonymy is intentional and rhetorical.

Checkpoint
What does the author say the testimony (shahada) will accomplish for the believer?
A) It will make them a sign for others.
B) It will be a means of deliverance in both worlds.
C) It will adorn them with jewels.
D) It will grant them gardens in Paradise.

Key Takeaway
The author's opening doxology establishes the theological foundation for the entire commentary, intertwining praise, testimony, and blessings with the key themes of the work.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the shahada daily, do you truly feel it as a 'means of deliverance in the two abodes' that the author describes, or has it become a routine phrase? What would it take for you to testify with that same awareness?
?
The author begins his commentary by praising God and blessing the Prophet. Is your own learning of Islamic theology similarly anchored in devotion, or do you treat it as mere academic study? How can you transform your study into an act of worship starting from this lesson?
?
The blessing on the Prophet includes 'his family and companions' for as long as the foundations of faith are established. Do you uphold respect and love for the Companions as a foundation of your creed? Is there any trace of negligence in your heart towards any of them?
