The Meaning of the Ba' in the Basmala
The commentator presents two opinions on the meaning of the ba' and refutes objections to the seeking-help interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scholarly opinions on the meaning of the ba' in the basmala and the commentator's refutation of the objection to the seeking-help interpretation.

Core Idea
The ba' in basmala indicates seeking help, and it is not disrespectful to seek help by Allah's name.

The commentator begins by noting that the ba' is a letter of meanings, requiring both a meaning and grammatical attachment. He then presents the dispute over its meaning: some say it indicates accompaniment for blessing, others say it indicates seeking help. Those who reject seeking help raise objections, but the commentator answers them decisively.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ba' as a letter of meanings
Read the opening of the passage to see why the ba' needs both a meaning and an attachment. This sets up the discussion.
2
Examine the two opinions on meaning
Identify the two opinions: accompaniment and seeking help. Notice that both are qualified as 'for blessing'.
The second opinion is the one the commentator defends.
3
Analyze the objections and refutations
Study the two objections raised by those who favor accompaniment. Then see how the commentator answers each one, using logical argument and hadith.
Focus on the hadith 'If you seek help, seek help from Allah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أشار الشارح بقوله: (أؤلف) إلى مُتعلّق الباء في البسملة ، وبقوله: (مستعينًا) إلى معنى الباء ، وفي كلّ واحدٍ من الأمرين مقال واختلاف ، ونحن نجمله لك، فنقول: هذه الباء حرف من حروف المعاني ؛ فلا بد من معرفة معناها ولا بد لها من متعلق ، فأما معناها فقد اختلف العلماء فيه؛ فقيل : هي للمصاحبة على وجه التبرُّك ، وقيل : هي للاستعانة على وجه التبرك أيضا، فأما الذين ذهبوا أنَّها للمصاحبة على وجه التبرُّك فإنَّما دعاهم إلى ذلك ادعاؤهم أَنَّ الاستعانة إنَّما تكون بذات الله ، لا باسم من أسمائه ، وادّعاؤهم أنَّ في جعل «الباء» للاستعانة سوء أدب ؛ لأنَّ «باء الاستعانة» تدخل على الآلة كما في قولك: كتبت بالقلم، ويلزم على ذلك جعل اسم الله تعالى مقصوداً لغيره لا لذاته، والجواب عن ذلك : أما عن الأوَّل: فإنَّه لا مانع من الاستعانة باسم الله تعالى كما يُستعان بذاته، وأما عن الثاني فيقال: إنَّ معنى الاستعانة باسم الله تعالى أنَّ الأمر المشروع فيه لا يتمُّ على الوجه الأكمل إلا باسمه سبحانه ، وكيف يكون في الاستعانة باسم الله تعالى سوء أدب ، وقد ورد في الحديث الأمر بالاستعانة به سبحانه ، وذلك قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «إذا استعنت فاستعن بالله ، وإذا عرفت هذا فلا تلتفت إلى ما قيل: إن سوء الأدب مع هذا المعنى المقصود بالاستعانة لا يزال موجودا. وأمَّا مُتعلّق الباء ، فقد اختلفوا فيه أيضاً، هل يُقدَّر فعلا أو اسمًا ؟ وعلى كل واحد منهما هل يقدر متقدماً أو متأخرًا ؟ وعلى كلّ من هذه الأوجه هل يقدَّر عاماً كأبتدئ وابتدائي، أو يقدَّرُ من جنس المشروع فيه فيقال: أؤلف أو تأليفي، إذا كان المشروع فيه تأليفا، وأشرب أو شُرْبي، إذا كنت شارِعاً في الشرب؟ وهلم جرا ، والأصحُ : أَنَّه يقدر فعلا ؛ لأنَّ الأصل في العمل للأفعال ، ويقدَّر متأخّرًا ، لكي لا يتقدم على اسم الله تعالى شيء لا في اللفظ ولا في التقدير ، ومن جنس المشروع فيه ، لأنَّ كلَّ شارع في شيء يجعل التسمية مبدأ لذلك الفعل. . . وفي هذا القدر كفاية .
The commentator indicated by his saying 'I compose' the attachment of the ba' in the Basmala, and by his saying 'seeking help' the meaning of the ba'. In each of these two matters there is discussion and disagreement, and we will summarize it for you: This ba' is a letter of meanings; so it is necessary to know its meaning and it must have an attachment. As for its meaning, scholars have differed: it is said it is for accompaniment in a blessed manner, and it is said it is for seeking help also in a blessed manner. Those who said it is for accompaniment in a blessed manner were led to that by their claim that seeking help is only with the Essence of Allah, not with a name of His names, and their claim that making the ba' for seeking help is bad manners because the ba' of seeking help enters on the instrument as in your saying 'I wrote with the pen', and that would necessitate making the name of Allah intended for something other than its essence. The answer to that: As for the first, there is no obstacle to seeking help by the name of Allah just as one seeks help by His essence. As for the second, it is said that the meaning of seeking help by the name of Allah is that the legislated action is not completed in the most perfect manner except by His name, the Exalted. How can there be bad manners in seeking help by the name of Allah when the hadith commands seeking help by Him, i.e., his saying, peace be upon him: 'If you seek help, seek help from Allah.' And if you understand this, do not pay attention to what is said that bad manners with this meaning intended by seeking help still exists. As for the attachment of the ba', they also differed: is it to be estimated as a verb or a noun? And in each of these two, is it estimated as preceding or following? And in each of these aspects, is it estimated as general like 'I begin' or 'my beginning', or is it estimated from the type of the legislated action, so one says 'I compose' or 'my composing' if the legislated action is composing, and 'I drink' or 'my drinking' if you are starting to drink? And so on. The correct view is that it is estimated as a verb, because the basis for action belongs to verbs, and it is estimated as following, so that nothing precedes the name of Allah neither in word nor in estimation, and from the type of the legislated action, because everyone who starts something makes the naming the beginning of that action. And in this amount is sufficient.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a detailed analytical commentary on the ba' in the basmala. The commentator first notes that the author's phrase 'I compose' points to the attachment of the ba', while 'seeking help' points to its meaning. He then explains two related issues: the meaning of the ba' and its grammatical attachment.

Regarding meaning, scholars hold two opinions: (1) it indicates accompaniment for blessing, and (2) it indicates seeking help, also for blessing. Those who favor accompaniment argue that seeking help is only with Allah's essence, not His names, and that using the ba' for seeking help would be disrespectful because the ba' of seeking help is used with instruments (e.g., 'I wrote with the pen'). The commentator refutes both arguments: first, seeking help by His name is permissible just as by His essence; second, the meaning of seeking help by His name is that the action is not completed perfectly except through His name, and the Prophet explicitly commanded seeking help from Allah. Thus, there is no disrespect.

Regarding the attachment of the ba', scholars differ on whether the hidden word (that the ba' attaches to) is a verb or a noun, whether it precedes or follows, and whether it is general or specific to the action. The correct view is that it is a verb, following the basmala, and specific to the action at hand. This ensures nothing precedes Allah's name in word or estimation, and the action begins with His name.

Key Terms
الباء
الاستعانة
المصاحبة
التبرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Using the name of Allah for seeking help is disrespectful.
Correction: The commentator argues that seeking help by Allah's name is permissible and even commanded in hadith, and it implies that the action is not completed perfectly without His name.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the correct interpretation of the ba' in the basmala?
A) Accompaniment for blessing
B) Seeking help for blessing
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The ba' in basmala indicates seeking help by Allah's name, which is permissible and supported by hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you genuinely sense that you are seeking Allah's help through His name, or has it become a mere habit? What would change if you intentionally meant isti'ana each time?
?
The hadith says, 'If you seek help, seek help from Allah.' In what areas of your life—work, relationships, struggles—do you still rely on your own strength instead of explicitly calling upon Allah's name for help?
?
Are you ever hesitant to use Allah's name in daily matters because you fear it might be disrespectful? How does the commentator's refutation of the 'sū' adab' objection challenge that feeling, and what step will you take this week to overcome it?

The Attachment of the Ba' in the Basmala
The commentator discusses the options for the grammatical attachment of the ba' and states the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly differences regarding the grammatical attachment of the ba' in the basmala and the correct view that it is a following verb specific to the action.

Core Idea
The ba' attaches to an implied verb of the same type as the action, placed after the basmala to honor Allah's name.

After clarifying the meaning, the commentator turns to the grammatical attachment of the ba'. Scholars disagree on the nature of the hidden word: is it a verb or a noun? Does it precede or follow? Is it general or specific to the action? The commentator presents all possibilities and then states the correct view, based on linguistic principles and reverence for Allah's name.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for an attachment
Note that the ba' as a preposition must attach to something. The commentator explores what that something is.
The attachment is an implied word, not explicitly stated.
2
Explore the three axes of disagreement
Work through each axis: verb vs noun, preceding vs following, general vs specific. Understand the possibilities and the reasoning behind each.
Use the examples given (e.g., 'I compose' vs 'my composing').
3
Understand the correct view
Read the part where the commentator states the correct view (الأصح) and the reasons: verb, following, specific to the action. Notice how this respects Allah's name by preventing anything from preceding it in word or estimation.
The reasoning is both grammatical (verbs are the basis for action) and reverential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the commentator, what is the correct view regarding the grammatical attachment of the ba' in the basmala?
A) It is attached to a following noun, like 'my beginning'

?

?

?

Reflect on one area where you have allowed something to come before His name—whether in your heart or actions—and decide today to restore His precedence.

The Divine Order of the Qur'an and the Basmala's Opening
The commentator explains the first reason for starting with basmala and the proof for tawqif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Qur'an's current arrangement is divinely ordained (tawqifi) and that the basmala was placed first by divine instruction, not because it was the first revealed verse.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's order is tawqifi; the basmala opens it by divine arrangement, not chronological revelation.

Sanusi begins his commentary by explaining why he starts with the basmala. The first reason is following the Qur'an's own beginning. This raises a question: Was the basmala the first revealed verse? The commentator clarifies it was not; rather, the arrangement of the Qur'an was taught by Jibril to the Prophet, who then instructed the Companions. This passage explains how we know the order is divinely set.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Focus on the distinction between first revelation and first in order. Note that the commentator mentions two reasons but only elaborates on the first.
The second reason is not detailed in this passage.
2
Understand the evidence for tawqif
Identify the proofs: Jibril instructed the Prophet on the placement of verses; the annual review in Ramadan; the Prophet's review with Zayd. This establishes the order as tawqifi.
Tawqifi means divinely ordained, not humanly arranged.
3
Grasp the distinction
Realize that the basmala being first does not automatically make it a verse of the Qur'an, just as saying bismillah before eating does not make it part of the food.
This sets up the next lesson on the three opinions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكرَ الشَّارِحُ سَببَيْن للابتداء باسم الله تعالى، الأول: الاقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، والمراد به القرآن الكريم، وليس معنى ذلك أن أول شيءٍ بُدئ بنزوله من القرآن الكريم هو البسملة؛ فإنَّ المعروفَ أَنَّ أَوَّل شيءٍ نزل به جبريل من القرآن الكريم هو قوله تعالى: ﴿اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ الآيات مِن أوّل سورة العلق، وإنَّما المقصود أن أوّل القرآن الكريم في ترتيبه الذي أمر به الرسول صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بإرشادِ جبريل هو ذلك؛ فإنَّ المعلوم أنَّ جبريل كان يقول للنبي عقب نزل شيءٍ مِن القرآن: موضع للنَّبيِّ في مكان كذا، بين وكذا، وكان الرسول الله يأمر أصحابه بذلك، وشيءٌ آخر يدل على ذلك، وهو: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان ينزل إليه جبريل في رمضانَ مِن كلّ عام فيُدارسه القرآن، وكان رسولُ الله يدارس زيداً بعد مدارسة جبريل؛ فثبت بهذا أنَّ القرآن الكريم كان مرتبا ترتيبا توقيفيا غير ترتيب نزوله، وهو على الوجه الذي بلغنا متواترا التواتر الذي يفيدُ القطع، ثمَّ إن ابتداء القرآن بالبسملة لا يستلزم أنَّها جزء منه، فإن الأكل وغيره من الأفعال تبدأ بالبسملة، وليست هي جزءاً من شيء منها، وفي هذه المسألة أقوال كثيرة ولكن أشهرها ثلاثة: الأوّل ـ وهو مذهب مالك - رحمه الله - أنها بعض آية من سورة النمل، لا غير. والثاني ـ وهو مذهب الشافعي - رحمه الله - أنها آية من سورة الفاتحة ومن كل سورة من سور القرآن. والثالث ـ وهو المعروف من مذهب الحنفية ـ أنها آية مستقلة نزلت ليفصل بها بين السور، وليست آية الفاتحة بخصوصها، ولا من سورة أخرى، غير النمل فإنها بعض آية من أثنائها.
The commentator mentioned two reasons for beginning with the name of Allah, the Exalted: First, following the Noble Book, meaning the Noble Qur'an. This does not mean that the first thing that began to be revealed from the Qur'an is the basmala; rather, it is known that the first thing Jibril brought down from the Noble Qur'an is His saying, the Exalted: 'Read in the name of your Lord who created' the verses from the beginning of Surat al-'Alaq. Rather, the intended meaning is that the beginning of the Noble Qur'an in its arrangement, which the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) commanded by the guidance of Jibril, is that. For it is known that Jibril used to say to the Prophet after the revelation of something from the Qur'an: 'Place for the Prophet in such and such place, between such and such,' and the Messenger of Allah would command his Companions to do that. Another thing indicates that: that the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, would have Jibril descend to him in Ramadan every year and he would review the Qur'an with him, and the Messenger of Allah would review with Zayd after reviewing with Jibril. Thus it is established that the Noble Qur'an was arranged in a divinely-ordained arrangement different from the order of its revelation, and it is in the manner that reached us through tawatur that yields certainty. Then, the beginning of the Qur'an with the basmala does not necessitate that it is a part of it, for eating and other actions begin with the basmala, and it is not a part of any of them. In this issue there are many statements, but the most famous are three: First — the view of Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) — that it is a part of a verse from Surat al-Naml, and nothing else. Second — the view of al-Shafi'i (may Allah have mercy on him) — that it is a verse of Surat al-Fatiha and of every sura of the Qur'an. Third — the well-known view of the Hanafis — that it is an independent verse revealed to separate between suras, and it is not a verse of al-Fatiha specifically, nor of any other sura, except al-Naml, as it is a part of a verse from within it.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage explains the two reasons for beginning the commentary with the basmala. The first reason is following the Noble Qur'an, which begins with the basmala in its arranged order. The commentator clarifies that the first revelation was from Surat al-'Alaq, not the basmala. The Qur'an's current arrangement was divinely taught by Jibril, who instructed the Prophet on the placement of verses. This is further evidenced by the annual review of the Qur'an between Jibril and the Prophet, and then between the Prophet and Zayd. Thus, the order is tawqifi (divinely ordained) and known via tawatur (mass transmission). The basmala being at the beginning does not mean it is necessarily a verse of the Qur'an; other actions also begin with the basmala without it being part of them. Then the commentator presents the three famous opinions on whether the basmala is a Qur'anic verse: (1) The Maliki view: it is only a part of a verse in Surat al-Naml, not elsewhere. (2) The Shafi'i view: it is a verse of al-Fatiha and every sura. (3) The Hanafi view: it is an independent verse separating suras, except in Surat al-Naml where it is part of a verse.

Key Terms
البسملة
ترتيب القرآن
توقيفي
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The basmala was the first verse revealed to the Prophet Muhammad.
Correction: The first revelation was the beginning of Surat al-'Alaq, not the basmala. The basmala was placed first in the compiled Qur'an by divine instruction.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the commentator provide for the Qur'an's order being tawqifi?
A) The basmala is written in all copies of the Qur'an
B) Jibril instructed the Prophet on the placement of verses
C) The basmala is always recited before reading
D) The Companions arranged the Qur'an by consensus

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's order is divinely ordained (tawqifi), and the basmala opens it by that order, not because it was first revealed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a surah, do you ever pause to consider that Allah Himself commanded its placement? How does this reality challenge the way you approach the Qur'an? Will you begin your next recitation with a conscious acknowledgment of its divine order?
?
The basmala opens the Qur'an as a reminder that every action should begin with His name. In what area of your life do you habitually start without turning to Allah? What concrete step will you take today to correct that, making basmala a lived practice, not just a ritual?
?
The proof for tawqif rests on Jibril's meticulous instructions and the annual review. If the order of the Qur'an is that precise, what does it say about your attention to the order of your own daily acts of worship? Will you realign your salah or recitation to match this divine precision, starting now?

Three Famous Opinions on the Qur'anic Status of Basmala
The commentator enumerates the three famous opinions on the basmala being a Qur'anic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the three major scholarly opinions on whether the basmala is a verse of the Qur'an: the Maliki, Shafi'i, and Hanafi views, and their respective positions.

Core Idea
Scholars differ: Malik says it is only in al-Naml; al-Shafi'i says it is a verse of every sura; Hanafis say it is an independent verse.

After establishing that the basmala's placement does not automatically make it part of the Qur'an, Sanusi presents the famous scholarly disagreement on its status. Three views are most notable: that of Malik, al-Shafi'i, and the Hanafis. This lesson examines each opinion and what it entails for recitation and prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the three opinions
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the three views. Note that the passage mentions these are the most famous among many opinions.
The opinions are listed in order: Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanafi.
2
Identify each opinion clearly
Define each: Maliki: basmala is only part of a verse in Surat al-Naml (Q.27:30). Shafi'i: it is a verse of al-Fatiha and of every sura. Hanafi: it is an independent verse revealed to separate suras, except in al-Naml where it is part of a verse.
The Hanafi view means it is a full verse by itself, but not part of any sura except al-Naml.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how these views affect whether one recites the basmala aloud in prayer, or whether it counts as a verse in recitation.
This shows how fiqh derives from such differences.

ذكرَ الشَّارِحُ سَببَيْن للابتداء باسم الله تعالى، الأول: الاقتداء بالكتاب العزيز، والمراد به القرآن الكريم، وليس معنى ذلك أن أول شيءٍ بُدئ بنزوله من القرآن الكريم هو البسملة؛ فإنَّ المعروفَ أَنَّ أَوَّل شيءٍ نزل به جبريل من القرآن الكريم هو قوله تعالى: ﴿اقْرَأْ بِاسْمِ رَبِّكَ الَّذِي خَلَقَ الآيات مِن أوّل سورة العلق، وإنَّما المقصود أن أوّل القرآن الكريم في ترتيبه الذي أمر به الرسول صلوات الله وسلامه عليه بإرشادِ جبريل هو ذلك؛ فإنَّ المعلوم أنَّ جبريل كان يقول للنبي عقب نزل شيءٍ مِن القرآن: موضع للنَّبيِّ في مكان كذا، بين وكذا، وكان الرسول الله يأمر أصحابه بذلك، وشيءٌ آخر يدل على ذلك، وهو: أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم كان ينزل إليه جبريل في رمضانَ مِن كلّ عام فيُدارسه القرآن، وكان رسولُ الله يدارس زيداً بعد مدارسة جبريل؛ فثبت بهذا أنَّ القرآن الكريم كان مرتبا ترتيبا توقيفيا غير ترتيب نزوله، وهو على الوجه الذي بلغنا متواترا التواتر الذي يفيدُ القطع، ثمَّ إن ابتداء القرآن بالبسملة لا يستلزم أنَّها جزء منه، فإن الأكل وغيره من الأفعال تبدأ بالبسملة، وليست هي جزءاً من شيء منها، وفي هذه المسألة أقوال كثيرة ولكن أشهرها ثلاثة: الأوّل ـ وهو مذهب مالك - رحمه الله - أنها بعض آية من سورة النمل، لا غير. والثاني ـ وهو مذهب الشافعي - رحمه الله - أنها آية من سورة الفاتحة ومن كل سورة من سور القرآن. والثالث ـ وهو المعروف من مذهب الحنفية ـ أنها آية مستقلة نزلت ليفصل بها بين السور، وليست آية الفاتحة بخصوصها، ولا من سورة أخرى، غير النمل فإنها بعض آية من أثنائها.
The commentator mentioned two reasons for beginning with the name of Allah, the Exalted: First, following the Noble Book, meaning the Noble Qur'an. This does not mean that the first thing that began to be revealed from the Qur'an is the basmala; rather, it is known that the first thing Jibril brought down from the Noble Qur'an is His saying, the Exalted: 'Read in the name of your Lord who created' the verses from the beginning of Surat al-'Alaq. Rather, the intended meaning is that the beginning of the Noble Qur'an in its arrangement, which the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) commanded by the guidance of Jibril, is that. For it is known that Jibril used to say to the Prophet after the revelation of something from the Qur'an: 'Place for the Prophet in such and such place, between such and such,' and the Messenger of Allah would command his Companions to do that. Conditions for Basmala Based on the Hadith
Classification of actions for basmala based on hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six categories of actions that determine whether basmala and hamdala are sunna, haram, makruh, or not recommended.

Core Idea
Basmala is only sunna for actions of religious importance.

This lesson unpacks a famous hadith that says any important matter not begun with Allah's name is deficient. The commentator explains what counts as an 'important matter' and lists the rulings for different categories of actions. Understanding these categories helps you apply the basmala correctly in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the classification system.
The passage begins with the second reason for basmala: acting on the hadith.
2
Identify the categories
Look for the six categories: trivial, inherently forbidden, forbidden due to a cause, inherently disliked, disliked due to a cause, and pure remembrance.
Each category has a specific ruling on basmala.
3
Understand the rulings
For each category, note whether basmala is sunna, haram, makruh, or not recommended. Also notice the exception for non-pure remembrance like Quran recitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسبب الثاني: أنه أرادَ العمل بالحديث النبوي الذي رواه ، وسنتكلم عليه فيما يلي .
الرّحيم» (١) أي: بداءة حقيقيَّة (۲) فَهُوَ أَبْتَرُ أو «أَقْطَعُ» أو «أَجْذَمُ» أي: ناقص
(1) البَالُ: الشَّأنُ والحال ، والمراد بـ (الأمر ذي البال»: الأمر الذي يهتم به شرعا بشرط ألا يكون مِن سَفَاسِف الأمور ، ولا مُحرماً ، ولا مكروها ، ولا ذكرا محضا ، ولا مما جعل له الشَّارِعُ مبدأ غير التسمية ، فإن كان من سَفَاسِف الأمور كلبس النعل والبصق والمخط فلا تُسن له البسملة ولا الحمدلة ، وإن كان محرَّماً لذاته كالسرقة والزّنا حرمت البسملة له والحمدلة عليه ، وإن كان محرَّما لعارض كالوضوء بماء مغصوب لم تحرم ، وإنْ كان مكروها لذاته كالنظر إلى فرج زوجته بلا حاجة كرهت ، وإن كان مكروها لعارض كأكل البصل لم تكره ، وإن كان ذكرًا محضاً كقولك: لا إله إلا الله ، لم تُسن له البسملة ، أما إن كان غير متمحض للذكر كتلاوة القرآن فإن البسملة تُسنُّ له، وإن كان الشَّارع قد جعل للأمر مبدأ غير البسملة والحمدلة كالصلاة فإن السنة تسن أن يبدأه المكلف بما جعله الشارع مبدأ كالتكبير.
The second reason: he intended to act by the prophetic hadith which he narrated, and we will discuss it later. 'The Most Merciful' (1) i.e., a real beginning (2) so it is abtar or aqta' or ajdham, i.e., deficient. (1) Al-bal means state and condition, and what is meant by 'a matter of importance' is the matter that is given importance legally, on condition that it is not of the trivialities, nor forbidden, nor disliked, nor pure remembrance, nor something for which the Lawgiver has appointed a beginning other than the tasmiyah. For if it is of the trivialities, like putting on sandals, spitting, and blowing the nose, then basmala and hamdala are not sunna for it. If it is forbidden in itself, like theft and adultery, basmala for it and hamdala upon it are forbidden. If it is forbidden due to an incidental cause, like ablution with usurped water, it is not forbidden. If it is disliked in itself, like looking at one's wife's private parts without need, it is disliked. If it is disliked due to an incidental cause, like eating onions, it is not disliked. If it is pure remembrance, like saying la ilaha illa Allah, basmala is not sunna for it. As for that which is not solely remembrance, like recitation of the Qur'an, then basmala is sunna for it. And if the Lawgiver has appointed for the matter a beginning other than basmala and hamdala, like prayer, then it is sunna that the responsible person begin it with what the Lawgiver made the beginning, like the takbir.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage provides the second reason for beginning with Allah's name: acting upon the prophetic hadith that says any important matter not begun with the name of Allah is deficient. The commentator elaborates on the hadith, defining 'matter of importance' (amr dhi bal) as an action that is legally significant, with several exclusions. These exclusions are: trivial acts (like putting on sandals, spitting), inherently forbidden acts (like theft, adultery), inherently disliked acts, and acts of pure remembrance (like dhikr). For each category, he gives the ruling: for trivial acts, basmala is not sunna; for inherently forbidden acts, it is haram; for forbidden due to a secondary cause, it is not haram; for inherently disliked, it is makruh; for disliked due to a secondary cause, it is not makruh; for pure remembrance, it is not sunna. However, for actions that are not purely remembrance, like Quran recitation, basmala is sunna. Finally, if the Lawgiver has prescribed a specific beginning for an act (like takbir for prayer), that takes precedence over basmala.

مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think basmala is recommended for every action without exception.
Correction: This passage shows basmala is only sunna for actions of religious importance, excluding trivial, forbidden, disliked, and pure remembrance acts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following actions would NOT have basmala as sunna?
A) Eating food
B) Reciting the Qur'an
C) Putting on sandals
D) Starting a marriage contract

Key Takeaway
Basmala is sunna only for important religious actions, not for trivial, forbidden, or purely remembrance acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are there moments in your worship—like prayer—where you mistakenly insert basmala despite the Prophet prescribing a different opening (takbir)? Will you commit to learning and following the specific beginnings set by the Lawgiver for each act of worship?

Method of Learning
beginner
5 min
Two Types of Beginning and Reconciling Hadith Variants
The commentator distinguishes between real and relative beginning and explains how scholars reconciled different wordings of the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and relative beginning (al-ibtida' al-haqiqi and al-ibtida' al-idafi) and how scholars reconciled different wordings of the hadith by applying this distinction.

Core Idea
Different hadith wordings are not contradictions; they are reconciled by distinguishing real vs. relative beginning.

This passage tackles an important methodological issue: how to handle multiple hadith variants that seem to conflict. The commentator first presents the variants, then introduces a key conceptual distinction—real versus relative beginning—which scholars used to harmonize the texts. This lesson guides you through that distinction and the scholars' reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith variants
Notice that the hadith has different wordings both at the beginning and at the end. The commentator lists three variants for the beginning and three for the end.
These variants are all authentic.
2
Understand the two types of beginning
Learn the definitions of al-ibtida' al-haqiqi (real beginning) and al-ibtida' al-idafi (relative beginning). The key is what, if anything, can precede the beginning.
3
Apply the distinction to the variants
See how scholars assigned the basmala variant to real beginning and the hamdala variant to relative beginning. Understand why the end variants needed no reconciliation.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why the scholars used the Qur'an as their basis for this classification. Reflect on how this approach respects all authentic narrations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أولاً أنَّ هذا الحديث يُروى برواياتٍ مختلفة ؛ فيروى في صدره: «كلّ أمرٍ ذِي بال لا يبدأ فيه الله» ، ويروى: كلُّ أمرٍ ذي بال لا يُبدأ فيه بالحمد الله»، ويروى في عَجُزه: «فهو أبتر» ، ويروى «فهو أقطع» ويروى: «فهو أَجْذَم».
واعلم ثانياً أن الابتداء على ضربين: الأول : الابتداء الحقيقي، والثاني: الابتداء الإضافي. فأما الابتداء الحقيقي فهو : الابتداء بالشيء أمام المقصود بحيث لا يتقدم على ذلك الشيء شيء ما ، وأما الابتداء الإضافي فهو : الابتداء بالشيء أمام المقصود سواء أتقدم على ذلك الشيء شيءٌ آخرَ غير المقصود أم لم يتقدم ؛ فهذا أعم من الأول، إضافيا ؛ لأنَّ الابتداء ليس ابتداءً وتقدما على كل شيء، بل هو ابتداء بالنسبة إلى شيء معين وهو المقصود ، إذا علمت هذين الأمرين فاعلم أن العلماء قد رأوا أنَّ الروايات التي وردت في عجز الحديث - وإن اختلفت في ألفاظها - مُتفقة في المعنى المراد؛ فإن المقصود بكل ألفاظها أنه ناقص وقليل البركة ، حتى إنه لو تم حسّاً لا يتم معنى، فلما رأوا أنَّ المعنى المراد لا يختلف باختلاف الألفاظ لم يبحثوا عن طريق يجمعون به بين الروايات المختلفة ، ولكنهم رأوا أن الاختلاف في الألفاظ التي ورد عليها صدر الحديث اختلاف جوهري ، وأنَّ العمل بإحدى الروايات يستلزم إهدار الرواية الأخرى لذلك بحثوا عن طريق للجمع بين الروايات المختلفة ، فأدَّاهم النظرُ إلى أن يحملوا رواية الابتداء ببسم الله ) على الابتداء الحقيقي ، وأنْ يحملوا رواية الابتداء (بالحمد لله ) على الابتداء الإضافي ويخصصوا رواية الابتداء بذكر الله بالبسملة والحمدلة ، فأما مستندهم في ذلك كله فهو القرآن الكريم ؛ لأن ترتيبه التوفيقي مبتدأ=.
Know firstly that this hadith is narrated with different versions: in its beginning it is narrated: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with Allah', and it is narrated: 'Every matter of importance that does not begin with praise of Allah', and in its end it is narrated: 'it is abtar', and it is narrated: 'it is aqta'', and it is narrated: 'it is ajdham'. Know secondly that beginning is of two types: the first is real beginning, and the second is relative beginning. As for real beginning, it is beginning with the thing before the intended object such that nothing precedes that thing at all. As for relative beginning, it is beginning with the thing before the intended object, whether another non-intended thing preceded that thing or not; this is more general than the first, being relative, because the beginning is not a beginning and precedence over everything, but rather it is a beginning relative to a specific thing, which is the intended object. When you know these two matters, then know that the scholars saw that the versions that came in the end of the hadith—although they differ in wording—agree in the intended meaning; for what is intended by all their wordings is that it is deficient and lacking blessing, such that even if it is complete in the sensory sense, it is not complete in meaning. When they saw that the intended meaning does not differ with difference of wording, they did not search for a way to reconcile the different versions. But they saw that the difference in the wordings of the beginning of the hadith is a substantial difference, and that acting on one version entails disregarding the other version. Therefore they searched for a way to reconcile the different versions, and consideration led them to interpret the version 'beginning with the name of Allah' as referring to real beginning, and to interpret the version 'beginning with praise of Allah' as referring to relative beginning, and to specify the version 'beginning with remembrance of Allah' as referring to basmala and hamdala. As for their basis in all that, it is the Noble Qur'an, because its tawqifi arrangement begins with...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage addresses the variants of the hadith and how scholars reconciled them. First, it notes that the hadith has different wordings both in its beginning (some say 'beginning with Allah', others 'beginning with praise of Allah') and in its end ('abtar', 'aqta'', 'ajdham'). Second, it introduces a key distinction: beginning (al-ibtida') is of two types—real (haqiqi) and relative (idafi). Real beginning means that nothing at all precedes the thing with which one begins. Relative beginning means the thing is the beginning only relative to the intended action, even if something else preceded it. This distinction is crucial. The scholars noted that the variations in the end of the hadith all mean the same thing—deficiency—so no reconciliation is needed. But the variations in the beginning are substantial: one wording specifies 'basmala', another 'hamdala'. Acting on one would ignore the other. So they reconciled by assigning 'beginning with basmala' to real beginning, 'beginning with hamdala' to relative beginning, and they considered 'beginning with dhikr Allah' to encompass both. Their ultimate proof for this interpretation is the Noble Qur'an, because its tawqifi arrangement begins with basmala.

Key Terms
ابتداء حقيقي
ابتداء إضافي
أبتر
أقطع
أجذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The different wordings of the hadith are contradictory and cannot all be authentic.
Correction: The passage shows that the differences are reconciled by distinguishing real vs. relative beginning for the first part, and by recognizing that the end variants all indicate the same meaning of deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did scholars reconcile the hadith variants that say 'beginning with basmala' and 'beginning with hamdala'?
A) They considered one version authentic and the other weak.
B) They applied the basmala version to real beginning and the hamdala version to relative beginning.
C) They said both mean the same thing so no reconciliation needed.
D) They ruled that only basmala is valid for all actions.

Key Takeaway
Different wordings of the hadith are not contradictory; they are reconciled by understanding real beginning (for basmala) and relative beginning (for hamdala), and by recognizing that end variants all indicate deficiency.

Rulings of Basmala and Hamdala for Various Actions
Classification of actions regarding basmala and hamdala rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to determine the ruling of saying basmala and hamdala for different types of actions based on their nature and Sharia classification.

Core Idea
Not every action deserves basmala—its ruling depends on the action's legitimacy and importance.

In this passage, the author explains that the basmala is not a one-size-fits-all formula. He provides a detailed classification of actions to show when saying 'Bismillah' is prescribed, disliked, forbidden, or simply not sunna. This lesson will guide you through each category, helping you apply the rules correctly.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'amr dhu al-bal'
Learn the definition and conditions that make an action worthy of basmala or hamdala.
The term refers to an affair given importance in Sharia, excluding certain types.
2
Identify the eight categories
Go through each category: trivial, haram in itself, haram due to something else, makruh in itself, makruh due to something else, purely remembrance, not purely remembrance, and actions with a prescribed beginning.
Each category has a specific ruling for basmala and hamdala.
3
Apply the rulings to examples
Use the examples given (like theft, looking at wife, reciting Quran) to see how the rule works.
This will help internalize the classification.
4
Recall the exception for prescribed beginnings
Note that some actions have a different prescribed opening, like prayer with takbir, and should start accordingly.
Basmala is not said for such actions.

البَالُ: الشَّأنُ والحال ، والمراد بـ (الأمر ذي البال»: الأمر الذي يهتم به شرعا بشرط ألا يكون مِن سَفَاسِف الأمور ، ولا مُحرماً ، ولا مكروها ، ولا ذكرا محضا ، ولا مما جعل له الشَّارِعُ مبدأ غير التسمية ، فإن كان من سَفَاسِف الأمور كلبس النعل والبصق والمخط فلا تُسن له البسملة ولا الحمدلة ، وإن كان محرَّماً لذاته كالسرقة والزّنا حرمت البسملة له والحمدلة عليه ، وإن كان محرَّما لعارض كالوضوء بماء مغصوب لم تحرم ، وإنْ كان مكروها لذاته كالنظر إلى فرج زوجته بلا حاجة كرهت ، وإن كان مكروها لعارض كأكل البصل لم تكره ، وإن كان ذكرًا محضاً كقولك: لا إله إلا الله ، لم تُسن له البسملة ، أما إن كان غير متمحض للذكر كتلاوة القرآن فإن البسملة تُسنُّ له، وإن كان الشَّارع قد جعل للأمر مبدأ غير البسملة والحمدلة كالصلاة فإن السنة تسن أن يبدأه المكلف بما جعله الشارع مبدأ كالتكبير
Al-bal: meaning affair and state, and what is meant by (al-amr dhu al-bal) is an affair that is given importance in Sharia, on condition that it not be among trivial matters, nor forbidden, nor disliked, nor purely remembrance, nor something for which the Lawgiver has appointed a beginning other than the tasmiya. So if it is among trivial matters like putting on sandals, spitting, and blowing the nose, then basmala and hamdala are not sunna for it. If it is forbidden in itself like theft and adultery, basmala for it and hamdala upon it are forbidden. If it is forbidden due to an extraneous factor like wudu with usurped water, it is not forbidden. If it is disliked in itself like looking at one's wife's private part without need, it is disliked. If it is disliked due to an extraneous factor like eating onions, it is not disliked. If it is purely remembrance like saying 'There is no god but Allah', basmala is not sunna for it. However, if it is not purely remembrance like reciting the Quran, then basmala is sunna for it. And if the Lawgiver has appointed for an affair a beginning other than basmala and hamdala, like prayer, then the sunna is for the responsible person to begin it with what the Lawgiver made the beginning, such as the takbir.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'amr dhu al-bal' and provides a detailed classification of actions in terms of the ruling of uttering basmala (saying 'Bismillah') and hamdala (saying 'Alhamdulillah'). The definition states that an 'amr dhu al-bal' is an affair that is given importance in Sharia, with the condition that it be neither a trivial affair, nor forbidden, nor disliked, nor purely remembrance, nor something for which the Lawgiver has specified a different opening formula. Then the author enumerates eight categories: (1) trivial actions, where basmala and hamdala are not sunna; (2) actions forbidden in themselves, where they are forbidden; (3) actions forbidden due to an extraneous factor, where they are not forbidden; (4) actions disliked in themselves, where they are disliked; (5) actions disliked due to an extraneous factor, where they are not disliked; (6) purely remembrance, where basmala is not sunna; (7) actions that are not purely remembrance, where basmala is sunna; (8) actions for which the Lawgiver has appointed a specific beginning, such as prayer with takbir, where the appointed beginning should be used instead. The passage emphasizes that the rulings depend on the nature of the action.

Reconciling Hadith Variants: Real vs. Relative Initiation
Explanation of hadith variants and the two types of initiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how scholars reconcile apparently conflicting hadith variants by distinguishing between real and relative initiation.

Core Idea
Hadith variants can be harmonized through careful conceptual distinctions.

This passage from the commentary introduces a famous hadith about beginning important matters. The hadith appears in multiple versions, with different wordings in both its beginning and its end. While the end variants are easily harmonized, the beginning variants pose a substantive challenge. To resolve it, the scholars introduce a distinction between two types of initiation: real and relative. Let's explore how they apply this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author first presents the hadith variants, then defines the two types of initiation, and finally explains the scholarly reconciliation.
Pay attention to the key terms: الابتداء الحقيقي and الابتداء الإضافي.
2
Understand the two categories of initiation
Identify the definitions of real initiation (nothing precedes the initiating act) and relative initiation (something may precede, but the initiation is relative to the intended matter). Notice that relative initiation is more general.
3
See how the reconciliation works
The scholars assign the basmala version to real initiation and the hamdala version to relative initiation. This resolves the apparent contradiction. Ask yourself why this is a valid solution.
The Quran's arrangement is cited as evidence: it begins with the basmala.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أولاً أنَّ هذا الحديث يُروى برواياتٍ مختلفة ؛ فيروى في صدره: «كلّ أمرٍ ذِي بال لا يبدأ فيه الله» ، ويروى: كلُّ أمرٍ ذي بال لا يُبدأ فيه بالحمد الله»، ويروى في عَجُزه: «فهو أبتر» ، ويروى «فهو أقطع» ويروى: «فهو أَجْذَم». واعلم ثانياً أن الابتداء على ضربين: الأول : الابتداء الحقيقي، والثاني: الابتداء الإضافي. فأما الابتداء الحقيقي فهو : الابتداء بالشيء أمام المقصود بحيث لا يتقدم على ذلك الشيء شيء ما ، وأما الابتداء الإضافي فهو : الابتداء بالشيء أمام المقصود سواء أتقدم على ذلك الشيء شيءٌ آخرَ غير المقصود أم لم يتقدم ؛ فهذا أعم من الأول، إضافيا ؛ لأنَّ الابتداء ليس ابتداءً وتقدما على كل شيء، بل هو ابتداء بالنسبة إلى شيء معين وهو المقصود ، إذا علمت هذين الأمرين فاعلم أن العلماء قد رأوا أنَّ الروايات التي وردت في عجز الحديث - وإن اختلفت في ألفاظها - مُتفقة في المعنى المراد؛ فإن المقصود بكل ألفاظها أنه ناقص وقليل البركة ، حتى إنه لو تم حسّاً لا يتم معنى، فلما رأوا أنَّ المعنى المراد لا يختلف باختلاف الألفاظ لم يبحثوا عن طريق يجمعون به بين الروايات المختلفة ، ولكنهم رأوا أن الاختلاف في الألفاظ التي ورد عليها صدر الحديث اختلاف جوهري ، وأنَّ العمل بإحدى الروايات يستلزم إهدار الرواية الأخرى لذلك بحثوا عن طريق للجمع بين الروايات المختلفة ، فأدَّاهم النظرُ إلى أن يحملوا رواية الابتداء ببسم الله ) على الابتداء الحقيقي ، وأنْ يحملوا رواية الابتداء (بالحمد لله ) على الابتداء الإضافي ويخصصوا رواية الابتداء بذكر الله بالبسملة والحمدلة ، فأما مستندهم في ذلك كله فهو القرآن الكريم ؛ لأن ترتيبه التوفيقي مبتدأ=
Know firstly that this hadith is transmitted in different versions: in its beginning it is narrated 'Every matter of significance in which God is not mentioned' and it is narrated 'Every matter of significance in which one does not begin with praise of God', and in its end it is narrated 'is cut off' and 'is severed' and 'is amputated'. Know secondly that initiation is of two types: the first is real initiation, and the second is relative initiation. As for real initiation, it is initiating with something before the intended object such that nothing precedes that thing. As for relative initiation, it is initiating with something before the intended object whether something else other than the intended object precedes that thing or not; this is more general than the first, relatively, because the initiation is not an initiation and precedence over everything, but rather it is initiation with respect to a specific thing which is the intended object. When you have known these two matters, know that the scholars have seen that the versions that came in the end of the hadith — though differing in their wording — agree in the intended meaning; for the intended meaning of all its wordings is that it is deficient and of little blessing, such that even if it is complete sensibly it is not complete meaningfully. They saw that the intended meaning does not differ due to differing wordings, so they did not search for a way to reconcile the different versions. However, they saw that the difference in the wordings that came in the beginning of the hadith is a substantive difference, and that acting upon one version necessitates neglecting the other version; therefore they searched for a way to reconcile the different versions. Their consideration led them to carry the version 'initiating with the name of God' on real initiation, and to carry the version 'initiating with praise of God' on relative initiation, and to restrict the version 'initiating with the mention of God' to basmala and hamdala. Their evidence for all of this is the Noble Quran, because its tawfiqi arrangement begins with...
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a methodological discussion of a famous hadith about beginning important matters. The author first notes that the hadith has multiple variants in both its beginning and its end. The end variants ('abtar', 'aqta', 'ajdham') all mean 'deficient' or 'cut off', so they are harmonizable. The beginning variants differ: one says 'begin with God' (bismillah), another says 'begin with praise of God' (alhamdulillah). This is a substantive difference. To reconcile them, the author introduces a distinction between two types of initiation: real initiation (al-ibtida' al-haqiqi) and relative initiation (al-ibtida' al-idafi). Real initiation is when nothing precedes the initiating act; relative initiation is when the initiating act may be preceded by something else not part of the intended matter. The scholars then carry the version with 'basmala' on real initiation, and the version with 'hamdala' on relative initiation. They base this on the Quran, whose arrangement begins with the basmala (i.e., Surah al-Fatiha begins with Bismillah, so that is a real initiation). The passage shows how careful reconciliation of hadith variants is done through conceptual distinctions.

Correction: The scholars reconcile them through conceptual distinctions: the end variants agree in meaning, and the beginning variants are harmonized by the categories of real and relative initiation.

Seemingly conflicting hadith variants can be harmonized by introducing precise conceptual distinctions, such as real vs. relative initiation.

Definition of Hamd and Relative Initiation
Definition of hamd and explanation of relative initiation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of hamd and its role as a relative initiation in contrast to the real initiation with basmala.

Core Idea
Hamd is praise for voluntary acts, used as a relative beginning.

This lesson unpacks the word 'hamd' and how it is used to open texts. The author distinguishes between a real beginning (with basmala) and a relative beginning (with hamd), resolving apparent contradictions in prophetic practices. We will explore the linguistic meaning and the concept of relative initiation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
The passage begins with a quoted line, then explains relative initiation.
2
Understand the linguistic definition of hamd
Identify the definition: praise with the tongue for a voluntary beautiful act. Note that it is specific to voluntary acts.
This distinguishes hamd from shukr, which can be for any blessing.
3
Distinguish real vs. relative initiation
Real initiation is with basmala; relative initiation is with hamd. The latter is placed before the intended matter but may be preceded by something else.
This reconciles hadiths that use basmala and those that use hamd.
4
Reflect on the commentator's role
The passage references the commentator's earlier and later statements to confirm the points.
The commentator's remarks reinforce the distinction.

The Order of Salam before Salat in the Text
Explains why salam precedes salat in the poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author placed 'salam' before 'salat' in the poem and how the word 'ma'a' subtly indicates the correct hierarchical rank.

Core Idea
Salam is mentioned first, but salat is still rank higher.

This lesson dives into a textual curiosity in the poem: why does the author say 'salam' before 'salat' when the Quran and custom order blessings first? The commentator explains the linguistic clues that resolve this apparent contradiction, showing how poetic necessity and word choice preserve doctrinal priority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then the literal translation. Notice the discussion about 'thumma' and the question raised about the order.
2
Understand the two interpretations of 'thumma'
The passage explains that 'thumma' can indicate resumption or sequential order. If sequential, it could be verbal order or hierarchical rank. Grasp this linguistic distinction.
The commentator notes that matters pertaining to the created are lower in rank than those pertaining to the Creator.
3
Analyze the answer using 'ma'a'
The answer invokes poetic necessity (ضرورة النظم) and the subtle use of 'ma'a' (with) attached to 'salat'. Since 'ma'a' normally accompanies the principal element, its attachment to 'salat' shows that salat is the main act, even though salam is mentioned first.
Example: 'The vizier came with the sultan' — sultan is principal.
4
Identify the key terms
Familiarize yourself with the key Arabic terms: ثم, ترتيب ذكري, ترتيب رتبي, ضرورة النظم, مع. These are essential for understanding the discussion.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Take the multiple-choice question to confirm you understood which indicator reveals the higher rank of salat.
Answer B is correct: the use of 'ma'a'.

وفي هذا المقام كلام كثير للعلماء الله تعالى ، وقد اجتزأنا لك منه باللُّباب، واقتطفنا مِن أزاهيرهم العَبَقةَ الشَّذَى ما لا تجده مجتمعاً في كتاب والله سبحانه يهدي من يشاء إلى سبيل الصواب. محي . (۱) تحتملُ (ثم) في هذه العبارة أن تكون للاستئناف، وتحتمل أن تكون: للعطف دالة على الترتيب؛ فإن كانت للترتيب احتمل أن يكون المراد الترتيب الذكري، وأن يكون المراد الترتيب الرتبي ، لأنَّ رتبة ما يتعلق بالمخلوق من الصلاة والسلام متأخرة ومتراخية عن رتبة ما يتعلق بالخالق من البسملة والحمدلة ، وقد بيّن الشارح معنى السَّلام ومعنى الصَّلاة، بقي أن يُقال: كيف قدم السَّلام على الصلاة مع أنَّ هذا مخالف لعرف الاستعمال، ولما ورد في قوله تعالى: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا صَلُّوا عَلَيْهِ وَسَلِّمُوا تَسْلِيمًا [الأحزاب: ٥٦] . والجواب على هذا: أنَّ ضرورة النظم ألجأَتْهُ إلى هذا التقديم، وقد احتاط لذلك فأشار إشارة خفيَّةٌ إلى أن رتبة السَّلام متأخرة عن رتبة الصَّلاة، وبيانه أنَّه أدخل «مع» على الصلاة، والمعروف أنَّ «مع» إنما تدخل على المتبوع لا التابع. ألا ترى أنك تقول: حضر الوزير مع السلطان ، ولا تقول: حضر السلطان
And in this context there is much speech from the scholars, may Allah have mercy on them. We have sufficed you with the core of it, and plucked from their fragrant blossoms what you will not find collected in a book. Allah Almighty guides whom He wills to the path of correctness. (1) The word 'thumma' in this expression can be for resumption, or it can be for conjunction indicating order. If it is for order, it may mean verbal order or hierarchical rank, because the rank of what pertains to the created (salat and salam) is later and remote from the rank of what pertains to the Creator (basmala and hamdala). The commentator has explained the meaning of salam and the meaning of salat. It remains to be asked: why was salam placed before salat when this contradicts customary usage and what is found in His saying: 'O you who have believed, send blessings upon him and greet him with peace'? The answer to this is that poetic necessity forced this presentation, and he guarded against it by subtly indicating that the rank of salam is later than the rank of salat. His explanation is that he inserted 'ma'a' (with) for salat, and it is known that 'ma'a' only accompanies the principal, not the subordinate. Do you not see that you say: 'The vizier came with the sultan', and you do not say: 'The sultan came'.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses a textual curiosity: the author of the poem mentioned 'salam' before 'salat' in the line 'thumma salamullahi ma'a salatihi', contrary to the normal order and the Quranic injunction to send blessings first then greetings. The commentator explains that the word 'thumma' may simply resume the discourse or indicate order. If it indicates order, it could be verbal order or hierarchical rank, noting that matters related to the created (salat and salam) are lower than those related to the Creator. The answer given is that poetic meter (ضرورة النظم) forced the presentation, but the author gave a subtle hint by using 'ma'a' (with) for salat, which indicates that salat is the principal element and salam is subordinate, since 'ma'a' is normally used with the main item. Thus, the actual rank of salat is higher than salam, even though salam is mentioned first.

The Objection about the Order of Salam and Salat
Objection and answer about poetic order of salam and salat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poet placed 'salam' before 'salat' and how the subtle use of 'ma'a' indicates the true rank.

Core Idea
Poetic necessity can override customary order, but wording reveals true rank.

In the previous lesson, the commentator explained the meanings of salam and salat. Now he addresses a potential objection: why does the poem mention salam before salat when both the Quran and customary usage place salat first? The answer lies in the demands of poetic meter and a clever grammatical clue.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the objection: why is salam before salat against expectation?
The objection cites Quran 33:56 which says 'sallu wa sallimu'.
2
Understand the answer
The answer is that the poetic structure required it. But the poet subtly indicated the true rank by using 'ma'a' with salat.
The word 'ma'a' typically follows the principal, not the subordinate.
3
Analyze the grammatical clue
Notice that 'ma'a' is used with salat, implying that salat is the principal and salam the subordinate, even though salam is mentioned first.
Similar to saying 'the vizier came with the sultan'.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how this shows that careful attention to wording can resolve apparent contradictions.

The word (thumma) in this phrase is probable to be for resumption, and probable to be for conjunction indicating order. If it is for order, then it is possible that what is meant is the order of mention, or the order of rank, because the rank of what pertains to the created of prayer and peace is later and far removed from the rank of what pertains to the Creator of the Basmalah and Hamdalah. And the commentator has explained the meaning of peace and the meaning of prayer. It remains to be said: How was peace mentioned before prayer, while this contradicts customary usage, and what is mentioned in His saying: 'O you who believe, send prayers upon him and greet him with a greeting of peace' [Ahzab: 56]? The answer to this is that the necessity of the poetic structure forced this presentation, and he took precautions by giving a subtle indication that the rank of peace is later than the rank of prayer. Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the order of 'salam' and 'salat' in the poem. The commentator first notes that 'thumma' could indicate resumption or conjunction with order. If it indicates order, it could be order of mention or order of rank, since the rank of created acts (salat and salam) is lower than that of acts pertaining to the Creator (Basmalah, Hamdalah). After explaining the meanings of salam and salat, the objection is raised: why is salam mentioned before salat when the Quranic order is 'sallu wa sallimu', and customary usage also places salat first? The answer is that the poetic meter required this order. The poet subtly indicated that salam's rank is actually lower than salat's by using 'ma'a' (with) with salat, because 'ma'a' indicates a following, not a leader—just as one says 'the vizier came with the sultan,' implying the vizier is subordinate. So salam is made to follow salat in rank even though it is mentioned first.

Refuting the Objection on the Sword: Three Grammatical Answers
Commentary refutes an objection about the timing of the Prophet's use of the sword.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how grammatical analysis of the conjunction 'fa' resolves the apparent conflict between the poem's wording and the historical timeline of the Prophet's mission.

Core Idea
The Arabic 'fa' is flexible: it can mean immediate or delayed sequence.

This passage tackles a subtle theological objection: does the poem's use of 'fa' (then) imply the Prophet used the sword immediately upon receiving revelation? History shows otherwise, so how can the poem be correct? The commentator provides three distinct grammatical answers, each deeply rooted in Arabic usage. As you read, notice how the objection arises from a strict reading of 'fa', and how the answers expand our understanding of Arabic particles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objection is presented. Why does the conjunction 'fa' seem to contradict the historical timeline?
The objection claims 'fa' forces immediate succession, but the Prophet's armed struggle began years later.
2
First answer: relativized immediacy
The commentator first argues that 'immediacy' (taʿqīb) is relative. Look at the examples: 'he came and greeted' vs. 'Zayd married and had a child'.
This shows that 'fa' can allow a delay, as long as no intervening event interrupts the logical sequence.
3
Second answer: ellipsis
The second answer posits a hidden middle step: the Prophet first came with tawḥīd, then guided through daʿwah, then guided with the sword. So 'fa' skips the intermediate phase.
This reconstruction preserves the order while respecting the historical gap.
4
Third answer: substitution of letters
The third answer asserts that 'fa' here functions like 'thumma' (then after a delay). Arabic particles can substitute for each other, as attested in Quran and poetry.
This is a bold grammatical claim, but the commentator cites examples to support it.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Reflect on how each answer uses a different linguistic principle. Which one do you find most convincing? How does this deepen your appreciation for Arabic as a theological medium?
The key is that theological coherence is preserved through careful grammatical analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذه العبارة لدفع اعتراض وردّ على عبارة المصنف، وحاصله أن جملة «أرشد الخلق لدين الحقِّ بسيفه» معطوف على جملة «جَاءَ بالتَّوحيد» التي هي صفةٌ للنَّبيِّ، وحرف العطف الذي هو الفاء يقتضي الترتيب والتعقيب، وحينئذ يكون المعنى: أن الرسول أرشد الخلق بالسيف عَقِيبَ مجيئه بالحقِّ من غير مُهْلَةٍ واقعة بين المجيء والإرشاد بالسيف، مع المعلوم أنَّ الإرشاد بالسيف الذي هو الجهاد لم يحصل إلا بعد مضي مدة طويلة إقامته [؟] بمكة بعد البعثة؛ لأن الجهادَ لم يُشرع إلَّا بعد الهجرة. ومُلَخَّصُ الجواب: أن التّعقيب في كلِّ شيءٍ بحَسَبِه، فتارة يكون من غير مدَّةٍ فاصلة بين وقوع المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، كما تقول: جاءَ فسلَّمَ، وتارة يكون بعد مضي مدة كقوله: تزوج زيد فولد له، وكقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِي أَخْرَجَ الْمَرْعَى فَعَلَهُ غُشَاء أَحْوَى) [الأعلى]. وهناك جوابان غير هذا الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح، وحاصل أولهما: أنَّ في الكلام محذوفاً؛ وتقديره في هذا المقام: جاء بالتوحيد فأرشد الناس بالدعوة سراً وجهرا فأرشد الناس بسيفه. وحاصل ثانيهما: أنَّ (الفاء) في هذه العبارة لا تدل على الترتيب والتعقيب كما هو في معنى الفاء، ولكنّها في هذه العبارة وما أشبهها تدلّ على الترتيب والتراخي، مثل: ثم، وقد عُرف أنَّ الحروف تتقارض، نعني أنَّ بعضها يقعُ في مكان بعض، وكما وقعت (ثُمَّ) في المكان الذي من حق الفاء أن تقع فيه في قول الشاعر: كَهَرُ الرُّدَيْنِي تَحْتَ العَجَاج جَرَى فِي الأَنَابِيبِ ثُمَّ اضْطَرَبْ، كذلك وقعت (الفاء) موقع (ثُمَّ) في الآية الكريمة، وفي المثال الذي ذكرناه قبلها، وفي كلام المصنف، ولذلك نظائر كثيرةٌ في العربية، فاعرف ذلك وتنبه له.
This statement is to repel an objection directed at the author's expression. Its summary is that the clause 'he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword' is coordinated with the clause 'he came with tawḥīd' which is a description of the Prophet, and the conjunction 'fa' requires order and immediacy. Then the meaning would be that the Messenger guided the creation with the sword immediately after his coming with the truth without any interval between the coming and the guidance with the sword, while it is known that guidance with the sword, which is jihād, only occurred after a long period of his stay [؟] in Mecca after the mission, because jihād was only legislated after the Hijra. The summary of the answer: that 'immediacy' (al-taʿqīb) is relative to each thing; sometimes there is no intervening period between the occurrence of the coordinated and the coordinator, as when you say: 'he came and greeted', and sometimes after a period, as in saying: 'Zayd married and had a child', and as in the Almighty's saying: 'And He who brought forth the pasture, then made it stubble darkish'. There are two other answers besides this answer mentioned by the commentator. The summary of the first: that there is an ellipsis in the statement; its reconstruction in this context: 'he came with tawḥīd, so he guided people by secret and open invitation, then he guided people with his sword.' The summary of the second: that the 'fa' in this expression does not indicate order and immediacy as is the usual meaning of 'fa', but rather it indicates order with delay, like 'thumma'. And it is known that letters can substitute for each other, meaning that one can occur in the place of another. Just as 'thumma' has occurred in a place where 'fa' should be in the poet's verse: 'The spear of Rudainī shook under the dust, stirred in the tubes, then trembled.' Similarly, 'fa' has occurred in the position of 'thumma' in the noble verse, in the example we mentioned before, and in the author's words. There are many analogies in Arabic, so know that and take heed.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection to the line 'he guided the creation to the religion of truth with his sword' from the poem. The objection is that the conjunction 'fa' implies immediacy, but historically the Prophet did not use the sword until after the Hijra, years after his mission began. The answer, first, is that 'immediacy' (taʿqīb) is relative: it can be immediate or after a period, as shown by Arabic usage and Quranic examples. Then two additional answers are given: (1) There is an ellipsis: the full meaning is that he first came with tawḥīd, then guided by secret and open invitation, then guided by the sword—so 'fa' joins the third step to the first, skipping the middle. (2) The 'fa' here does not mean immediate sequence but rather delayed sequence, like 'thumma', and this substitution of letters is known in Arabic. The passage demonstrates a nuanced understanding of Arabic grammar to protect the theological coherence of the poem.

Correction: The Arabic 'fa' can indicate either immediate or delayed sequence depending on context, and it can even substitute for 'thumma' (delayed sequence) as shown in Quran and poetry.

The Particle Fa Indicating Delay (Tarākhī) in Kalam Arguments
Commentary explaining that the particle fa can indicate delayed sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic particle 'fa' can sometimes indicate a delayed sequence (tarākhī) rather than immediate sequence, and how this insight resolves apparent chronological contradictions in theological statements.

Core Idea
Fa doesn't always mean 'immediately after'—it can mean 'later, after a delay.'

The particle 'fa' (so/then) normally indicates immediate succession, but in some contexts—including key kalam arguments—it can indicate a delayed sequence, similar to 'thumma' (then after a while). This grammatical insight helps defend the coherence of statements about the Prophet's mission and other theological claims.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the normal function of 'fa'
Learn that 'fa' usually implies immediate sequence, as in 'he came and greeted.' This is its default meaning.
2
See examples of 'fa' with delay
Examine examples like 'Zayd married and a child was born' and the Quranic verse about pasture, where 'fa' implies a delay.
These show that 'fa' can be used for events separated by time.
3
Explore the two alternative explanations
The commentator gives two answers to reconcile the delayed sense in 'jā'a bi-l-tawḥīdi fa-arshadahum': one posits an omitted phrase, the other argues for substitution of letters.
The first answer suggests an implied sequence of da'wah then jihad. The second invokes the principle of letter substitution.
4
Understand the concept of letter substitution
Learn that letters like 'fa' and 'thumma' can sometimes exchange roles in Arabic, as shown by a poetic example and the Quranic verse.
This principle is called 'taqāruḍ al-ḥurūf'.
5
Apply to the theological argument
Realize that this grammatical flexibility allows the statement to refer to two distinct phases of guidance (da'wah and jihad) without contradiction.
The lesson shows how grammar serves theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوع المعطوف والمعطوف عليه، كما تقول : جاءَ فسلَّمَ، وتارة يكون بعد مضي مدة كقوله: تزوج زيد فولد له، وكقوله تعالى: وَالَّذِي أَخْرَجَ الْمَرْعَى فَعَلَهُ غُشَاء أَحْوَى ) [الأعلى]. وهناك جوابان غير هذا الجواب الذي ذكره الشارح ، وحاصل أولهما: أنَّ في الكلام محذوفاً ؛ وتقديره في هذا المقام: جاء بالتوحيد فأرشد الناس بالدعوة سراً وجهرا فأرشد الناس بسيفه. وحاصل ثانيهما : أنَّ (الفاء) في هذه العبارة لا تدل على الترتيب والتعقيب كما هو في معنى الفاء، ولكنّها في هذه العبارة وما أشبهها تدلّ على الترتيب والتراخي، مثل: ثم، وقد عُرف أنَّ الحروف تتقارض ، نعني أنَّ بعضها يقعُ في مكان بعض، وكما وقعت (ثُمَّ) في المكان الذي من حق الفاء أن تقع فيه في قول الشاعر: محي . المشهور كَهَرُ الرُّدَيْنِي تَحْتَ العَجَاج جَرَى فِي الأَنَابِيبِ ثُمَّ اضْطَرَبْ كذلك وقعت (الفاء) موقع (ثُمَّ) في الآية الكريمة ، وفي المثال الذي ذكرناه قبلها ، وفي كلام المصنف، ولذلك نظائر كثيرةٌ في العربية ، فاعرف ذلك وتنبه له .
The occurrence of the conjoined and the conjoined upon, as you say: 'He came and greeted.' And sometimes it is after a period of time, like his saying: 'Zayd married and a child was born to him,' and like the saying of the Most High: 'And who brought out the pasture, then made it black stubble' [al-A'la]. And there are two answers other than this answer mentioned by the commentator. The gist of the first is that there is an omission in the speech, and its estimation in this context is: 'He brought tawhid, so he guided the people through da'wah secretly and publicly, then he guided them with his sword.' The gist of the second is that the particle 'fa' in this expression does not indicate order and immediacy as is in the meaning of fa, but it indicates in this expression and similar ones order and delay (tarākhī), like 'thumma'. And it is known that letters can substitute for each other, meaning that some occur in the place of others, just as 'thumma' occurred in the place where fa has the right to occur in the poet's saying: 'The spear of Rudaynī under the dust ran in the tubes, then (thumma) shook.' Thus also 'fa' occurred in the position of 'thumma' in the noble verse, and in the example we mentioned before it, and in the speech of the author. There are many analogies in Arabic, so know that and pay attention.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical defense of the use of the particle 'fa' in the matn. It begins by explaining that the normal use of 'fa' indicates immediate sequence, as in 'he came and greeted.' However, it can also occur after a period of time, as in 'Zayd married and a child was born to him,' and in the Qur'anic verse about pasture. The commentator then presents two alternative answers to explain the 'fa' in the phrase 'jāʾa bi-l-tawḥīdi fa-arshadahum' (he brought tawhid and guided them). The first answer posits an omitted phrase: that he first guided through da'wah and later through the sword. The second answer argues that the 'fa' here does not indicate immediate sequence but rather a delayed sequence (tarākhī), like the letter 'thumma'. The commentator supports this by noting that letters can replace each other, citing a poetic example where 'thumma' is used in a context where 'fa' would be expected, and conversely 'fa' occurs in the position of 'thumma' in the noble verse and in the author's speech. He concludes that there are many such analogies in Arabic, urging the reader to understand this point.

Key Terms
الفاء (al-fāʾ)
التراخي (al-tarākhī)
التعقيب (al-taʿqīb)
تقارض الحروف (taqāruḍ al-ḥurūf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the particle 'fa' always implies immediate sequence.
Correction: The passage demonstrates that 'fa' can indicate delayed sequence (tarākhī), especially when context requires it, and this is supported by examples from Arabic usage and the principle of letter substitution.

Checkpoint
According to the second answer in the passage, what does the particle 'fa' indicate in the phrase 'جاء بالتوحيد فأرشدهم'?
A) Immediate sequence (taʿqīb)
B) Delayed sequence (tarākhī)
C) Causality
D) Concession

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' can indicate a delayed sequence (tarākhī) in Arabic, and recognizing this resolves apparent chronological contradictions in theological texts.

?
This lesson shows that even a single particle can carry nuanced meaning. Think of a relationship where someone misunderstood your tone or timing. Using 'Rab' for Other than Allah: Legal vs. Linguistic Permissibility
Discusses legal permissibility and distinction from linguistic permissibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'Rab' cannot be used for creatures legally, even if used in Arabic poetry, and the crucial distinction between legal and linguistic permissibility.

Core Idea
Legal rulings are not derived from poetry, but from revelation.

This passage addresses a nuanced theological and legal issue: Is it permissible to call someone other than Allah 'Lord'? The author explains that while the word may be used linguistically, legally it is exclusive to Allah. He refutes objections from pre-Islamic poetry by distinguishing between legal and linguistic evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two forms of 'Rab' discussed
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that the author discusses two forms: 'Rab' without 'al' and without construct, and 'Rab' with 'al'.
The ruling for both forms is that it is not permissible to use for other than Allah.
2
Understand why previous divine laws are not binding
The passage begins by stating that previous laws are not binding for Muslims if contradicted by Shari'ah. This is a methodological principle.
This sets the stage for rejecting evidence from previous nations.
3
Analyze the refutation of poetic evidence
The author quotes two poets: al-Nabigha and al-Harith. He argues that their use of 'Rab' for other than Allah does not prove legal permissibility because legal rulings are not taken from poets, especially Jahili poets.
This refutes the objection that the word was used in pre-Islamic poetry.
4
Grasp the difference between legal and linguistic permissibility
The author clarifies that if the question were about linguistic permissibility, poetic evidence would be valid. But here we are concerned with legal permissibility (جواز شرعي), which is based on revelation, not language.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the ruling.
5
Reflect on the implications
Why does this matter? It shows that Islamic theology and law are based on divine revelation, not cultural or linguistic usage. Even if a word is used in Arabic, it may be restricted in a legal sense.

In our law, it is not lawful for us if there is in our law what prevents it. And as for using it singularly without 'al' and without construct, the correct view is that it is not permissible to apply it to other than Allah the Exalted; and as for the saying of al-Nabigha al-Dhubyani: 'We love al-Nu'man until we attain him, may I be ransomed from a Lord of my new and old property' – it does not indicate permissibility, because legal rulings are not taken from poets, especially the Jahiliyyah. And as for using it singularly with 'al', it is not permissible to apply it to other than Him the Exalted, and as for the saying of al-Harith ibn Hilliza concerning al-Mundhir ibn Ma' al-Sama': 'He is the Lord and the Witness over the Day of al-Hiyarayn, and the affliction is affliction' – it is like the saying of al-Nabigha mentioned earlier; it is not valid as a basis for a legal ruling. Yes, if the intended meaning is the permissibility of applying this word or that according to linguists to such-and-such, then it is valid to use the saying of al-Nabigha and al-Harith as evidence. Indeed, the saying of the like of them in that regard is more appropriate to be used as evidence. And it should be that the intended meaning of permissibility in this context is legal permissibility, not linguistic permissibility.

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal ruling (حكم شرعي) on using the word 'رب' (Lord) for creatures. It begins by noting that previous divine laws are not binding for Muslims if contradicted by the Shari'ah. Then it discusses two forms: (1) 'رب' without the definite article or in construct state (مفرداً مجرداً من أل ومن الإضافة) – the correct ruling is that it is not permissible to apply to anyone other than Allah. Objections from pre-Islamic poetry (e.g., al-Nabigha and al-Harith) are invalid because legal rulings are not derived from poets, especially Jahili poets. (2) 'رب' with the definite article (الرب) – also exclusive to Allah. The passage argues that if the intended permissibility is linguistic (جواز لغوي), then quoting such poetry is valid, but the context here is legal permissibility (جواز شرعي). Thus, the poems do not prove legal permissibility. This distinction between linguistic and legal permissibility is crucial.
