Definition of a Companion (Sahabi) and Its Conditions
Defines Sahabi and explains conditions of meeting, faith, and dying Muslim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a Sahabi and the conditions of companionship, including the nuances of meeting, faith, and dying as a Muslim.

Core Idea
A Companion is defined by meeting the Prophet with faith, not by physical vision or adulthood.

This lesson unpacks the classical definition of a Companion (Sahabi). The author explains that companionship is established through meeting the Prophet with faith and dying as a Muslim, with key nuances about the conditions. You will see how grammatical analysis of the word 'sahb' clarifies the terminology, and why the condition of discernment (tamyiz) is not required for companionship itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of Sahabi
Focus on the core sentence: 'والصحابي: من لقيه صلى الله عليه وسلم مُميَّزاً مؤمناً به ومات على الإسلام'. Identify the three conditions: meeting, discernment, faith, and dying Muslim.
The author later clarifies that discernment is not a condition for companionship.
2
Understand the grammatical discussion of 'sahb'
The passage differentiates between 'sahb' as a plural (al-Akhfash) and as a collective noun (Sibawayh). This affects how we understand the term 'Sahabi' as a group.
This is a technical point from Arabic grammar relevant to hadith terminology.
3
Analyze the condition of meeting (liqa')
Meeting is broad: it includes not just sitting together but even seeing from a distance, and it includes the blind through physical presence. Why does the author prefer 'laqiya' over 'ra'a'?
This ensures blind Companions like Ibn Umm Maktum are included.
4
Examine faith and dying Muslim
Learn why dying upon Islam is a condition for the continuance of companionship, not its essence. See examples of those excluded (apostates) and included (returnees).
The passage also discusses the case of those who believed in the Prophet before his prophethood.
5
Review the benefits and Malikite position
The definition has practical benefits: naming someone as Companion and establishing marital suitability. Note the Maliki view does not require continuous companionship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وَ) على صَحْبه أي: أصحابه، والصحابي: من لقيه صلى الله عليه وسلم مُميَّزاً مؤمناً به ومات على الإسلام؛ تفسير الصحب بالأصحاب يوهم أنه يعتبره جمعاً لصاحب، وهو مذهب أبي الحسن الأخفش، زعم أن صحباً جمع صاحب، ونظيره شرب وشارب، وتجر وتاجر، والتحقيق قول سيبويه إمام أهل هذه الصناعة، ذهب إلى أن صحباً اسم جمع لصاحب؛ لأنه على زنة من زنات جموع التكسير المعروفة. ومن هنا تعلم أن قول النحاة: (اسم الجمع: ما ليس له من لفظه، بل له واحد من معناه) مبني على الغالب والكثير، وأن مدار الفرق بين الجمع واسم الجمع على أن يكون على زنة من أوزان الجموع أولاً، فإن كان على زنتها فهو جمع، وإن لم يكن فهو اسم جمع. قوله: (من لقيه) والمراد باللقاء: ما هو أعم من المجالسة، والمماشاة، ووصول أحدهما إلى الآخر، وإن لم يكالمه، ويدخل فيه برؤية أحدهما الآخر، سواء عرفه أو لا، والتعبير بـ(لقي) أولى من قول بعضهم رأى؛ لأنه يخرج ابن أم مكتوم ونحوه من العميان، وهم صحابة بلا تردد، ولقي في هذا التعريف كالجنس. قوله: (مميزاً) ليس بقيد على الصحيح، وقد عرفوا محمد بن أبي بكر صحابياً مع ولادته قبل وفاته صلى الله عليه وسلم بثلاثة أشهر، والتمييز إنما يشترط لتلقي الرواية فقط، فيدخل من بالتمر من الصبيان، والمجنون المحكوم بإسلامه فيما يظهر والنائم، فلا يشترط قصد ذلك الشخص الاجتماع، ولا معرفة أحدهما الآخر، و(مميزاً) حال من فاعل (لقي). قوله: (مؤمناً به) خرج من لقيه كافراً، ولو آمن به بعده كالتنوخي رسول هرقل. قوله: (به) هذا فصل ثان، يخرج من لقيه مؤمناً بغيره من الأنبياء، لكن هل يخرج من لقيه مؤمناً بأنه سيبعث ولم يدرك البعثة، فيه نظر! قلت: مال شيخ الإسلام إلى اعتبار لقيه له بعد نبوته، ونقل عن كلام ابن حجر ما يدل عليه. وقوله: (ومات على الإسلام) هذا ليس بشرط في أصل الصحبة، بل شرط في دوامها، غاية الأمر أن من زاده أراد تعريف الصحابي الذي ثبتت له الصحبة ولم تنقطع، ويخرج بالتعريف الأول من اجتمع به مؤمناً ومات مرتداً، كعبد الله بن خطل فليس بصحابي، ويدخل في التعريفين من عاد للإيمان وتوفي مؤمناً كعبد الله بن أبي سرج، وفائدتها: التسمية؛ فيسمى صحابياً، والكفاءة؛ فيكون كفؤاً لبنت الصحابي، قال بعضهم: قلت ومن ذلك جعل من اجتمع به تابعياً، وعدم حنث الحالف على أنه صحابي، واشتهر أنها لا تعود عند المالكية.
(And) upon his companions, i.e. his companions. And the companion (al-sahabi) is: whoever met him (peace be upon him) discerning, believing in him, and died upon Islam. The interpretation of 'sahb' as 'ashab' suggests that he considers it a plural of 'sahib', and this is the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Akhfash: he claimed that 'sahb' is a plural of 'sahib', and its analog is 'sharb' and 'sharib', and 'tajr' and 'tajir'. But the verified view is the statement of Sibawayh, the imam of the people of this craft: he held that 'sahb' is a collective noun (ism jam') for 'sahib', because it is on one of the known patterns of broken plurals. From this you learn that the grammarians' statement: 'A collective noun is that which does not have a singular from its own wording, but rather has a singular from its meaning' is based on the prevalent and frequent, and that the criterion for distinguishing between a plural and a collective noun is whether it is on one of the patterns of plurals or not; if it is on a plural pattern, it is a plural, and if not, it is a collective noun. (His saying: 'whoever met him') and the meaning of meeting (liqa') is more general than sitting together, walking together, and one reaching the other even if he did not speak to him, and it includes seeing one another, whether he knows him or not. The expression 'met' (laqiya) is better than the saying of some: 'saw' (ra'a), because it excludes Ibn Umm Maktum and the like of the blind, and they are undoubtedly Companions. 'Laqiya' in this definition is like a genus. (His saying: 'discerning') is not a condition according to the correct view, and they have recognized Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr as a Companion even though he was born three months before the Prophet's death. Discrimination (tamyiz) is only required for receiving narration. So it includes children who are breastfed, the insane person who is judged to be Muslim apparently, and the sleeping person. The intention of that person to meet, nor the knowledge of one another, is not required. (His saying: 'believing in him') excludes whoever met him as a disbeliever, even if he believed later like al-Tanukhi, the messenger of Heraclius. (His saying: 'in him') this is a second class: it excludes whoever met him believing in another prophet. But does it exclude whoever met him believing that he will be sent but did not witness the sending? There is consideration! I say: Shaykh al-Islam inclined to consider meeting him after his prophethood, and he transmitted from the statement of Ibn Hajar what indicates it. (His saying: 'and died upon Islam') this is not a condition for the essence of companionship, but rather a condition for its continuance. At most, whoever adds it intends the definition of a Companion whose companionship is established and not cut off. And by the first definition, it excludes whoever met him believing and died an apostate, like 'Abdullah ibn Khatal, so he is not a Companion. And both definitions include whoever returned to faith and died a believer, like 'Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh. Its benefit is: naming; so he is called a Companion, and suitability; so he is suitable for the daughter of a Companion. Some said: I say, and from that is making whoever met him a follower (tabi'i), and the non-breach of an oath of one who swore he is a Companion. And it is well-known that it does not return according to the Malikis.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'صحابي' (Companion) and provides a detailed commentary on its conditions. It begins by stating the lexical meaning: 'صحبه' means 'أصحابه' (his companions). Then the formal definition is given: a Companion is anyone who met the Prophet (PBUH) while being discerning (مميز), believing in him (مؤمناً به), and died upon Islam (مات على الإسلام). The commentary then breaks down each element. First, the word 'صحب' is grammatically analyzed: contrary to al-Akhfash's view that it is a plural, the verified view of Sibawayh is that it is a collective noun (اسم جمع) because it does not have a singular of the same form. The distinction between a plural and a collective noun is based on whether the word follows a known plural pattern. Second, 'meeting' (لقاء) is broad: it includes more than just sitting together; it includes seeing, even without speaking. The word 'لقي' is preferred over 'رأى' because it includes the blind like Ibn Umm Maktum, who are undeniably Companions. Third, 'discernment' (تمييز) is not a condition for companionship itself; it is only required for transmitting hadith. Thus, infants, the insane, and the sleeping can be Companions if they met the Prophet in a state of apparent Islam. Fourth, 'believing in him' excludes those who met him as disbelievers, even if they later believed. Also, believing in another prophet is insufficient. There is a debate about those who believed he would be sent but did not witness the sending. Fifth, 'dying upon Islam' is not a condition for the essence of companionship (أصل الصحبة) but for its continuance; it excludes apostates like 'Abdullah ibn Khatal. The benefits of this definition include the ability to call a person a Companion and to establish suitability (كفاءة) in marriage. The passage notes that the Malikis hold that such suitability is not lost if the Companion dies.

اسم جمع
لقاء
تمييز
إيمان
إسلام
صحبة
كفاءة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A Companion must have seen the Prophet and been an adult of sound mind.
Correction: The passage clarifies that blind people, infants, and even the insane can be Companions if they met the Prophet with faith and died Muslim.

Checkpoint

Origin of 'Wa Ba'd' and the Start of Defining Knowledge
Author explains 'wa ba'd' and begins defining knowledge of religion's principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the origin and function of the phrase 'wa ba'd' in classical Arabic texts and how it introduces the definition of knowledge of religious principles.

Core Idea
'Wa ba'd' is a conditional transition, not a mere filler.

This lesson opens with a common rhetorical device in Islamic scholarship: 'wa ba'd'. The author traces its roots to a conditional construction and then pivots to the central subject—knowledge of the fundamentals of religion. You will also see the beginning of a definition from a renowned scholar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author moves from explaining 'wa ba'd' to defining knowledge.
The passage is split into two distinct parts: the origin of 'wa ba'd' and the definition of knowledge.
2
Focus on the explanation of 'wa ba'd'
Identify the key points: its function (transition), its origin ('amma ba'd'), and the evidence (the frequent 'fa' showing conditional sense).
The author uses a technical linguistic argument here.
3
Understand the transition to knowledge
Notice how the explanation of 'wa ba'd' sets the stage for the main topic: 'knowledge of the fundamental of religion'. The author defines this as knowledge of principles and beliefs.
This links the rhetorical introduction to the subject matter.
4
Examine the beginning of the definition of knowledge
The passage ends with the start of a quote from al-Raghib on knowledge. Reflect on why the author might begin defining knowledge here, even though the definition continues later.
This definition is incomplete in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أصل كلمة (وبعد) وتعريف مطلق العلم والجهل
وبَعْدُ فَالْعِلْمُ بِأَصْلِ الدِّينِ مُحَيَّمْ يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّبيير
(وَبعدُ) يُؤتى بها للانتقال من أسلوب إلى آخر، وأصلها: «أما بعد» بدليل لزوم الفاء في حيزها غالبًا، لتضمن «أما» معنى الشرط، والأصل: مهما يكن من شيء بعد البسملة وما بعدها.
(فَالْعِلْمُ بِأَصْلِ الدِّينِ) أي: بأصوله وقواعده، وهي العقائد الآتي بيانها.
قال الراغب: العلم:
Origin of the word 'wa ba'd' and the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance. 'Wa ba'd' is used for transition from one style to another, and its origin is 'amma ba'd', evidenced by the frequent occurrence of 'fa' in its scope, because 'amma' contains the meaning of condition. The original (meaning) is: whatever thing after the basmalah and after it. 'Knowledge of the fundamental of religion' means its principles and rules, which are the beliefs to be explained later. Al-Raghib said: Knowledge:
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading about the origin of the phrase 'wa ba'd' and the definition of absolute knowledge and ignorance. It then quotes a line from the poem: 'And after, knowledge of the fundamental of religion is important and needs clarification.' The author explains that 'wa ba'd' is used to transition from one style of speech to another, and its original form is 'amma ba'd', because 'amma' implies a conditional sense, often requiring 'fa' afterward. The original meaning is 'whatever thing after the basmalah and after it'. Then the author explains 'knowledge of the fundamental of religion' as knowledge of its principles and rules, which are the beliefs to be detailed later. The passage ends by beginning a quote from al-Raghib about knowledge, but this definition is cut off at the end of the page.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'wa ba'd' is just a conventional filler with no real meaning.
Correction: The author shows 'wa ba'd' derives from 'amma ba'd' and carries a conditional sense, making it a meaningful rhetorical transition.

Checkpoint
What is the original form of 'wa ba'd' according to the author?
A) 'Amma ba'd
B) 'Ba'd al-basmalah
C) 'Wa ba'd fa
D) 'Mahma yakun min shay'

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wa ba'd' originates from 'amma ba'd' with a conditional meaning, and it serves to transition to the subject of knowledge of religious principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you treat the opening words of a book or lecture as mere formality without pausing to consider their deeper significance? This lesson reveals that even 'wa ba'd' carries a conditional weight. What might you be missing in your own study because you skip over seemingly small phrases?
?
The author defines 'knowledge of the fundamental of religion' as something that requires clarification. Are you actively seeking to understand the principles of your faith, or do you rely on surface-level knowledge that doesn't condition your heart and actions? What step will you take today to deepen that understanding?
?
The origin of 'amma ba'd' lies in the condition (shart): what follows is contingent. In your own life, do you live with awareness that your iman is conditioned on fulfilling obligations and avoiding prohibitions? Where are you treating your faith as unconditional, and how will you correct that starting now?

The Transitional Phrase 'وبعد' and Its Origin
The author explains the usage and derivation of the word 'وبعد' in kalam texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rhetorical function of 'وبعد' in classical kalam texts and its derivation from 'أما بعد'.

Core Idea
Every 'وبعد' in kalam traces back to 'أما بعد', a conditional transition.

When you open a classical kalam text, you often encounter the word 'وبعد' right after the basmalah. It signals a shift from the opening to the main discourse. In this lesson, we examine the commentary on this common transitional phrase, exploring its original form and the reason for the accompanying 'فاء'. Understanding this small word helps you see the careful rhetorical structure of classical scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
The passage includes a matn line and commentary. Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow.
Notice the unclear word 'مُحَيَّمْ' – it may be 'مُحَتَّم' (certainly).
2
Identify the function of 'وبعد'
The commentary explicitly says it is used for transition from one style to another. Recognize it as a discourse marker.
3
Examine the derivation
The origin is 'أما بعد'. The evidence is the frequent occurrence of 'فاء' after it, because 'أما' implies a condition.
Conditional phrases often require a response with 'فاء'.
4
Reflect on the implied original phrase
The commentator suggests the full phrase is 'مهما يكن من شيء بعد البسملة وما بعدها' (whatever there is after the basmalah and what follows it).
This shows the conditional structure underlying the transition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبَعْدُ فَالعِلْمُ بأصلِ الدِّينِ مُحَيَّمْ[؟] يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّبيير. (وَبعدُ) يُؤتى بها للانتقال مِن أسلوب إلى آخر، وأصلها: «أما بعد» بدليل لزوم الفاء في حيّزها غالباً، لتضمن «أما» معنى الشَّرط، والأصل: مهما يكن من شيء بعد البسملة وما بعدها.
And after, knowledge of the foundation of the religion is certainly [?] in need of clarification. (And 'after') is used for transition from one style to another, and its origin is 'as for after' due to the necessary occurrence of the fa in its scope often, because 'as for' implies the meaning of condition, and the original: whatever is of a thing after the basmalah and what follows it.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains the rhetorical term 'وبعد' (wa ba'd) used in classical Arabic texts. It begins by quoting the main text: 'وبَعْدُ فَالعِلْمُ بأصلِ الدِّينِ مُحَيَّمْ[؟] يَحْتَاجُ لِلتَّبيير' — meaning that after the opening, knowledge of the fundamentals of religion is certainly in need of clarification. Then the commentary on the word 'وبعد' states that it is used for transitioning between sections. Its origin is 'أما بعد' (ammā ba'd), and the presence of the particle 'fā' in its scope is evidence, because 'أما' carries a conditional meaning. The original full phrase would be: 'مهما يكن من شيء بعد البسملة وما بعدها' — whatever there is after the basmalah and what comes after it. This explains the standard opening of many classical works.

Key Terms
وبعد
أما بعد
الفاء
الانتقال

C) أما بعد
D) من بعد

Key Takeaway
The transitional word 'وبعد' in kalam texts is derived from 'أما بعد' and functions to shift the discourse, often requiring a 'فاء' due to its conditional origin.

Individual Obligation to Know Creed with a General Proof
Establishes individual obligation for creed with a general proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowing the core creedal beliefs with at least a general proof is an individual obligation upon every Muslim.

Core Idea
Knowing your creed with a general proof is a personal duty.

The author begins by establishing the fundamental obligation of learning Islamic creed. He explains that the Lawgiver has made it obligatory for every responsible person to know each belief with at least a general proof. A verse from the Quran is cited as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses the term 'muḥayyam' to indicate obligation.
2
Identify the ruling
Identify the ruling (individual obligation) and who is obligated (every legally responsible person).
3
Note the evidence
Find the Quranic verse cited as proof and consider its relevance.
The verse commands knowledge of Allah's oneness.
4
Understand 'general proof'
Understand that at this stage, 'general proof' simply means having a proof without requiring ability to elaborate or defend it.
The detailed explanation of 'general proof' comes later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية. وقوله : (مُحيَّمُ) خَبَر «فالعلم الواقع مُبتدأ(۱) محتم اسم مفعول ، ومعناه أوجبه الشارع ، ولم يرخّص في تركه، فيجب على كلّ مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوبًا عينيا معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا ، ودليل ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] وأما معرفة العقائد بالأدلة التفصيلية ففرض كفاية، ومعناه أنَّه يجب على أهل كلّ ناحية يشقُ الوصولُ منها إلى غيرها أن يكون فيهم من يعرف العقائد بالدليل التفصيلي؛ لأنَّه ربَّما طرأت شُبهة فيدفعها، هذا هو الذي عليه جمهرة علماء العلماء من أوجب على كلِّ مكلَّف ذكرًا كان أو أنثى وجوبا عينيَّا أنْ يعرف كلَّ عقيدة بدليلها التَّفصيلي. وليس هذا بمرضي، فقد ضيق هؤلاء ما وسع الله تعالى على عباده ، وجعلوا في دين الله الحَرَجَ والجَهْد الجاهد، والله أرأف بعباده مِن أنْ يُكلِّفهم ما لا يطيقون . فإن قلت: فما حد الدليل الإجمالي ، وحد الدليل التفصيلي؟ حتّى أعرِفَ ما يجب على عامة الخلق ، وما يجب على خاصتهم. فالجواب عن ذلك: أنَّ الدَّليل الإجمالي هو الذي يعجز صاحِبه عن تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه الشُّبه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو ما يقدر صاحبه على تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه من الشبه ، من وخذ لك مثلا تتضح منه هذه الحقيقة، إذ قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجود الله تعالى؟ فقلت: الذي يدل على وجود الله تعالى هو هذا العالم، ولم تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجود الله ، أو عرفتها ، ولكنك لم تعرف كيف ترد على ما يُورَدُ على هذا الدليل مِن الشُّبه ، فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل الإجمالي ، وأما إذا كنت تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجوده سبحانه ، وكنت مع ذلك تستطيع أن تدفع كلَّ شُبهة يورِدُها المُورِدُ على هذا الدليل فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل التفصيلي. أمَّا مَن حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف العلماء فيه : أمؤمن هو أم كافرُ ؟ وإذا كان مؤمناً فهل هو عاص باكتفائه بالتقليد أم غير عاص ؟ والذي صححه الجمهرة من العلماء: أنَّه مؤمن ، ثمَّ إن كان بحيث يقدر على النظر فهو عاص بتركه ما قدر عليه، وإن كان بحيث لا يقدر على النظر فهو غير عاص لأنَّه ما ترك شيئًا إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله؛ وقال جماعة: هو مؤمن غير عاص مطلقا ، أي : سواء أقدر على النظر أم لم يقدر ، وقال قوم: هو مؤمن عاص مطلقا،
The ruling of learning the fundamentals of religion by general and detailed proofs. And His saying: (muḥayyam) is the predicate of 'al-ʿilmu al-wāqiʿu' as subject (1). It is a passive participle, meaning the Lawgiver has made it obligatory and did not permit leaving it. So it is an individual obligation upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed with a proof, even general. The evidence for that is His saying: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (47:19). As for knowing the creeds by detailed proofs, it is a communal obligation, meaning it is necessary for the people of every region from which reaching others is difficult that there be among them those who know the creeds by detailed proof, because perhaps a doubt may arise and he would repel it. This is the position of the majority of scholars: that it is an individual obligation upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed by its detailed proof. But this is not satisfactory, for these people have narrowed what Allah has made wide for His servants, and have placed hardship and great difficulty in the religion of Allah, and Allah is more compassionate to His servants than to burden them with what they cannot bear. If you say: What then is the limit of the general proof and the limit of the detailed proof, so that I may know what is obligatory upon the common people and what is obligatory upon their elite? The answer is: The general proof is that which its possessor is unable to elaborate and repel the doubts that come against it. As for the detailed proof, it is that which its possessor is able to elaborate and repel the doubts that come against it. For example, to clarify this reality: If it is said to you: What is the proof for the existence of Allah? And you say: That which indicates the existence of Allah is this world, and you do not know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to the existence of Allah, or you know it but you do not know how to repel the doubts that are brought against this proof, then you are knowledgeable of the general proof. But if you know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to His existence, and you can repel every doubt that the objector brings against this proof, then you are knowledgeable of the detailed proof. As for one who has memorized the creeds by taqlīd, the scholars differed concerning him: is he a believer or a disbeliever? And if he is a believer, is he sinful for contenting himself with taqlīd or not sinful? The position that the majority of scholars have deemed correct is that he is a believer. Then if he is able to engage in reflection (naẓar), he is sinful for abandoning what he was capable of. But if he is unable to reflect, he is not sinful, because he did not leave anything except that he was incapable of attaining it. Some said: He is a believer and not sinful absolutely, i.e., whether he is able to reflect or not. And some said: He is a believer and sinful absolutely.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling (ḥukm) of learning the fundamentals of religion (uṣūl al-dīn). It begins by explaining that the word 'muḥayyam' in the poem indicates the obligation imposed by the Lawgiver. Therefore, it is an individual obligation (farḍ ʿayn) upon every responsible person to know each creed (ʿaqīda) with at least a general proof (dalīl ijmālī). The author cites the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' as evidence. Then he contrasts that with knowledge by detailed proof (dalīl tafṣīlī), which is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya): each region must have someone capable of elaborating and defending the proofs against doubts. The author notes that some scholars require detailed proof for every individual, but he criticizes this as making the religion difficult and narrowing what Allah has made wide. The passage then clarifies the definitions: a general proof is one that its possessor cannot elaborate or defend against doubts; a detailed proof is one he can. An example using the proof of God's existence from the world illustrates the distinction. Finally, the passage addresses the case of one who follows taqlīd (blind imitation) in creed. The majority view is that such a person is a believer; if he was capable of reflection (naẓar) and omitted it, he is sinful; if incapable, he is not. There are also absolute views: some say he is not sinful at all, others say he is sinful absolutely.

Key Terms
وجوب عيني
دليل إجمالي

Key Takeaway
Every Muslim is personally responsible for knowing their creed with at least a general proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that knowing your creed with at least a general proof is an individual obligation upon you. Which of your beliefs do you currently hold without any proof? What is one concrete step you will take this week to acquire that proof?
?
The verse commands you to 'know that there is no god but Allah.' Have you ever truly known the meaning of this declaration with certainty, or have you merely accepted it as a family tradition? If you were asked to defend it with a proof, could you?
?
Since this obligation applies to you personally, regardless of your gender or background, what has prevented you from fulfilling it until now? Will you continue to delay, or will you set aside time today to learn one creed with its proof?

Detailed Proof: A Communal Obligation and a Critique
Discusses communal obligation of detailed proof and critiques stricter view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that knowing creed with detailed proof is a communal obligation and why some scholars' view making it individual is criticized.

Core Idea
Detailed proof is a community duty, not a personal one.

The author contrasts the individual obligation of general proof with the communal obligation of detailed proof. He then criticizes those who require detailed proof from every individual, arguing that this goes against the ease of Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the shift from individual to communal obligation.
2
Understand communal obligation
Understand that detailed proof is a farḍ kifāya: each region must have someone capable of defending the proofs.
The reason given is to repel doubts.
3
Identify the critique
Identify the author's criticism of those who make detailed proof an individual obligation.
He says they 'have narrowed what Allah has made wide'.
4
Reflect on the principle
Reflect on the Islamic principle of ease and not burdening people beyond their capacity.

حكم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية. وقوله : (مُحيَّمُ) خَبَر «فالعلم الواقع مُبتدأ(۱) محتم اسم مفعول ، ومعناه أوجبه الشارع ، ولم يرخّص في تركه، فيجب على كلّ مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوبًا عينيا معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا ، ودليل ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] وأما معرفة العقائد بالأدلة التفصيلية ففرض كفاية، ومعناه أنَّه يجب على أهل كلّ ناحية يشقُ الوصولُ منها إلى غيرها أن يكون فيهم من يعرف العقائد بالدليل التفصيلي؛ لأنَّه ربَّما طرأت شُبهة فيدفعها، هذا هو الذي عليه جمهرة علماء العلماء من أوجب على كلِّ مكلَّف ذكرًا كان أو أنثى وجوبا عينيَّا أنْ يعرف كلَّ عقيدة بدليلها التَّفصيلي. وليس هذا بمرضي، فقد ضيق هؤلاء ما وسع الله تعالى على عباده ، وجعلوا في دين الله الحَرَجَ والجَهْد الجاهد، والله أرأف بعباده مِن أنْ يُكلِّفهم ما لا يطيقون . فإن قلت: فما حد الدليل الإجمالي ، وحد الدليل التفصيلي؟ حتّى أعرِفَ ما يجب على عامة الخلق ، وما يجب على خاصتهم. فالجواب عن ذلك: أنَّ الدَّليل الإجمالي هو الذي يعجز صاحِبه عن تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه الشُّبه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو ما يقدر صاحبه على تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه من الشبه ، من وخذ لك مثلا تتضح منه هذه الحقيقة، إذ قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجود الله تعالى؟ فقلت: الذي يدل على وجود الله تعالى هو هذا العالم، ولم تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجود الله ، أو عرفتها ، ولكنك لم تعرف كيف ترد على ما يُورَدُ على هذا الدليل مِن الشُّبه ، فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل الإجمالي ، وأما إذا كنت تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجوده سبحانه ، وكنت مع ذلك تستطيع أن تدفع كلَّ شُبهة يورِدُها المُورِدُ على هذا الدليل فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل التفصيلي. أمَّا مَن حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف العلماء فيه : أمؤمن هو أم كافرُ ؟ وإذا كان مؤمناً فهل هو عاص باكتفائه بالتقليد أم غير عاص ؟ والذي صححه الجمهرة من العلماء: أنَّه مؤمن ، ثمَّ إن كان بحيث يقدر على النظر فهو عاص بتركه ما قدر عليه، وإن كان بحيث لا يقدر على النظر فهو غير عاص لأنَّه ما ترك شيئًا إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله؛ وقال جماعة: هو مؤمن غير عاص مطلقا ، أي : سواء أقدر على النظر أم لم يقدر ، وقال قوم: هو مؤمن عاص مطلقا،

As for the detailed proof, it is that which its possessor is able to elaborate and repel the doubts that come against it. Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is detailed proof a communal obligation?
A) Because it is too difficult for individuals
B) To ensure that in each region someone can repel doubts
C) Because the Quran commands it
D) Because the majority of scholars said so

Key Takeaway
Detailed proof is a communal obligation; requiring it from every individual goes against the ease of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author warns against making religion narrow. Is there a practice or belief you have insisted on with yourself or others that Allah did not make obligatory? What would change if you let go of that hardship?
?
This lesson places responsibility on the community to have defenders of the faith. Are you contributing to that communal duty, or are you leaving it to others? What is one step you can take this week to support those who carry this responsibility?

Defining General and Detailed Proof with an Example
Defines general and detailed proof with a concrete example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definitions of general and detailed proof and how they apply using the example of proving God's existence.

Core Idea
General proof is inability to elaborate; detailed proof is ability to defend against doubts.

The author provides clear definitions for general and detailed proof, answering a hypothetical question. He illustrates the distinction with an example using the proof of God's existence from the world, showing what each level entails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the definitions and example.
2
Understand the definitions
Note that general proof is defined by inability to elaborate and defend; detailed proof by ability to do so.
The same proof (e.g., the world) can be known generally or in detail.
3
Follow the example
Follow the example of proving God's existence. Notice the two levels: knowing the world indicates God vs. being able to explain and defend that indication.
4
Apply to yourself
Consider which level you are at for various creedal proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية. وقوله : (مُحيَّمُ) خَبَر «فالعلم الواقع مُبتدأ(۱) محتم اسم مفعول ، ومعناه أوجبه الشارع ، ولم يرخّص في تركه، فيجب على كلّ مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوبًا عينيا معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا ، ودليل ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] وأما معرفة العقائد بالأدلة التفصيلية ففرض كفاية، ومعناه أنَّه يجب على أهل كلّ ناحية يشقُ الوصولُ منها إلى غيرها أن يكون فيهم من يعرف العقائد بالدليل التفصيلي؛ لأنَّه ربَّما طرأت شُبهة فيدفعها، هذا هو الذي عليه جمهرة علماء العلماء من أوجب على كلِّ مكلَّف ذكرًا كان أو أنثى وجوبا عينيَّا أنْ يعرف كلَّ عقيدة بدليلها التَّفصيلي. وليس هذا بمرضي، فقد ضيق هؤلاء ما وسع الله تعالى على عباده ، وجعلوا في دين الله الحَرَجَ والجَهْد الجاهد، والله أرأف بعباده مِن أنْ يُكلِّفهم ما لا يطيقون . فإن قلت: فما حد الدليل الإجمالي ، وحد الدليل التفصيلي؟ حتّى أعرِفَ ما يجب على عامة الخلق ، وما يجب على خاصتهم. فالجواب عن ذلك: أنَّ الدَّليل الإجمالي هو الذي يعجز صاحِبه عن تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه الشُّبه، وأما الدليل التفصيلي فهو ما يقدر صاحبه على تقريره ودفع ما يرد عليه من الشبه ، من وخذ لك مثلا تتضح منه هذه الحقيقة، إذ قيل لك : ما الدليل على وجود الله تعالى؟ فقلت: الذي يدل على وجود الله تعالى هو هذا العالم، ولم تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجود الله ، أو عرفتها ، ولكنك لم تعرف كيف ترد على ما يُورَدُ على هذا الدليل مِن الشُّبه ، فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل الإجمالي ، وأما إذا كنت تعرف وجه دلالة وجود العالم على وجوده سبحانه ، وكنت مع ذلك تستطيع أن تدفع كلَّ شُبهة يورِدُها المُورِدُ على هذا الدليل فأنت حينئذ عالم بالدليل التفصيلي. أمَّا مَن حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف العلماء فيه : أمؤمن هو أم كافرُ ؟ وإذا كان مؤمناً فهل هو عاص باكتفائه بالتقليد أم غير عاص ؟ والذي صححه الجمهرة من العلماء: أنَّه مؤمن ، ثمَّ إن كان بحيث يقدر على النظر فهو عاص بتركه ما قدر عليه، وإن كان بحيث لا يقدر على النظر فهو غير عاص لأنَّه ما ترك شيئًا إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله؛ وقال جماعة: هو مؤمن غير عاص مطلقا ، أي : سواء أقدر على النظر أم لم يقدر ، وقال قوم: هو مؤمن عاص مطلقا،
The ruling of learning the fundamentals of religion by general and detailed proofs. And His saying: (muḥayyam) is the predicate of 'al-ʿilmu al-wāqiʿu' as subject (1). It is a passive participle, meaning the Lawgiver has made it obligatory and did not permit leaving it. So it is an individual obligation upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed with a proof, even general. The evidence for that is His saying: 'Know that there is no god but Allah' (47:19). As for knowing the creeds by detailed proofs, it is a communal obligation, meaning it is necessary for the people of every region from which reaching others is difficult that there be among them those who know the creeds by detailed proof, because perhaps a doubt may arise and he would repel it. This is the position of the majority of scholars: that it is an individual obligation upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed by its detailed proof. But this is not satisfactory, for these people have narrowed what Allah has made wide for His servants, and have placed hardship and great difficulty in the religion of Allah, and Allah is more compassionate to His servants than to burden them with what they cannot bear. If you say: What then is the limit of the general proof and the limit of the detailed proof, so that I may know what is obligatory upon the common people and what is obligatory upon their elite? The answer is: The general proof is that which its possessor is unable to elaborate and repel the doubts that come against it. As for the detailed proof, it is that which its possessor is able to elaborate and repel the doubts that come against it. For example, to clarify this reality: If it is said to you: What is the proof for the existence of Allah? And you say: That which indicates the existence of Allah is this world, and you do not know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to the existence of Allah, or you know it but you do not know how to repel the doubts that are brought against this proof, then you are knowledgeable of the general proof. But if you know the aspect of indication of the existence of the world to His existence, and you can repel every doubt that the objector brings against this proof, then you are knowledgeable of the detailed proof. As for one who has memorized the creeds by taqlīd, the scholars differed concerning him: is he a believer or a disbeliever? And if he is a believer, is he sinful for contenting himself with taqlīd or not sinful? The position that the majority of scholars have deemed correct is that he is a believer. Then if he is able to engage in reflection (naẓar), he is sinful for abandoning what he was capable of. But if he is unable to reflect, he is not sinful, because he did not leave anything except that he was incapable of attaining it. Some said: He is a believer and not sinful absolutely, i.e., whether he is able to reflect or not. And some said: He is a believer and sinful absolutely.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling (ḥukm) of learning the fundamentals of religion (uṣūl al-dīn). It begins by explaining that the word 'muḥayyam' in the poem indicates the obligation imposed by the Lawgiver. Therefore, it is an individual obligation (farḍ ʿayn) upon every responsible person to know each creed (ʿaqīda) with at least a general proof (dalīl ijmālī). The author cites the verse 'Know that there is no god but Allah' as evidence. Then he contrasts that with knowledge by detailed proof (dalīl tafṣīlī), which is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāya): each region must have someone capable of elaborating and defending the proofs against doubts. The author notes that some scholars require detailed proof for every individual, but he criticizes this as making the religion difficult and narrowing what Allah has made wide. The passage then clarifies the definitions: a general proof is one that its possessor cannot elaborate or defend against doubts; a detailed proof is one he can. An example using the proof of God's existence from the world illustrates the distinction. Finally, the passage addresses the case of one who follows taqlīd (blind imitation) in creed. The majority view is that such a person is a believer; if he was capable of reflection (naẓar) and omitted it, he is sinful; if incapable, he is not. There are also absolute views: some say he is not sinful at all, others say he is sinful absolutely.

Key Terms
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think general proof is a weaker or insufficient proof, but the passage shows it is still valid knowledge, just without elaboration ability.
Correction: General proof is sufficient for obligation; the key difference is the ability to elaborate and defend, not the validity of the proof itself.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a general proof from a detailed proof?
A) A general proof is from the Quran, detailed from reason.
B) A general proof cannot be elaborated or defended against doubts, while a detailed proof can.
C) A general proof is for common people, detailed for scholars only.
D) A general proof is sufficient for salvation, detailed for perfection.

Key Takeaway
General proof is valid knowledge but lacks elaboration; detailed proof includes the ability to defend against doubts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you were challenged about your belief in God and you could not give a clear, reasoned response? What kept you from preparing a detailed answer?
?
If you only have general proof for your faith, are you comfortable leaving it at that, or does this lesson stir you to seek deeper understanding? What specific doubt will you work to be able to defend this week?
?
The passage distinguishes between the common person and the elite. Do you truly desire to be among the elite who can elaborate and defend the truth? What concrete step will you take today to move from general to detailed proof?

The Ruling on Taqlid in Creed
Discusses differing scholarly opinions on taqlid in creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly differences regarding a person who merely imitates (taqlīd) in creedal matters and the condition of sinfulness.

Core Idea
Taqlid in creed is acceptable for the incapable, but sinful for those able to reflect.

The author concludes by addressing a common scenario: a person who accepts creedal beliefs through blind imitation (taqlīd) without personal reflection. He presents the different scholarly opinions on their faith and sinfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation about taqlid. Notice the three opinions mentioned.
2
Identify the majority view
Identify the view that the majority of scholars deemed correct: the person is a believer, but sin depends on ability to reflect.
3
Understand the condition
Note that sinfulness is conditional: if able to reflect (naẓar) and does not, he is sinful; if incapable, not sinful.
4
Compare the two absolute views
Compare the two other views: one says not sinful absolutely, the other says sinful absolutely.

Ruling on Learning Creeds with Summary and Detailed Evidences
Definition of rulings on learning creed with summary and detailed proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between personal and communal obligation in learning creedal proofs, and why the author critiques making detailed knowledge obligatory on everyone.

Core Idea
Know the creed with a proof, but detailed proofs are for specialists, not everyone.

This passage from al-Kharashi's commentary on Jawharat al-Tawḥīd clarifies the ruling on learning the principles of religion. The author explains that every Muslim must know their creed with at least a summary proof, but detailed proofs are a communal responsibility. He then critiques those who impose detailed knowledge on all, arguing that Allah does not burden souls beyond their capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms muḥattam, farḍ ʿayn, farḍ kifāyah, and the two types of evidences (summary and detailed).
These are foundational for understanding obligation.
2
Understand the first ruling
The passage begins with the personal obligation to know every creed with a summary proof. Note the evidence from the Quran.
3
Understand the second ruling
Then it explains that detailed proofs are a communal obligation, required only in some to defend against doubts.
The author clarifies that this is the mainstream view.
4
Examine the author's critique
Finally, the author criticizes those who make detailed knowledge a personal obligation, calling it a constriction of what Allah has made wide.
This shows the author's methodological stance.

حكم تعلم أصول الدين بالأدلة الإجمالية والتفصيلية وقوله : (مُحيَّمُ) خَبَر «فالعلم الواقع مُبتدأ(۱) (۱) محتم اسم مفعول ، ومعناه أوجبه الشارع ، ولم يرخّص في تركه، فيجب على كلّ مكلَّف من ذكر وأنثى وجوبًا عينيا معرفة كل عقيدة بدليل ولو إجماليًّا ، ودليل ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللهُ ﴾ [محمد : ۱۹] وأما معرفة العقائد بالأدلة التفصيلية ففرض كفاية، ومعناه أنَّه يجب على أهل كلّ ناحية يشقُ الوصولُ منها إلى غيرها أن يكون فيهم من يعرف العقائد بالدليل التفصيلي؛ لأنَّه ربَّما طرأت شُبهة فيدفعها، هذا هو الذي عليه جمهرة العلماء من أوجب على كلِّ مكلَّف ذكرًا كان أو أنثى وجوبا عينيَّا أنْ يعرف كلَّ عقيدة بدليلها التَّفصيلي. وليس هذا بمرضي، فقد ضيق هؤلاء ما وسع الله تعالى على عباده ، وجعلوا في دين الله الحَرَجَ والجَهْد الجاهد، والله أرأف بعباده مِن أنْ يُكلِّفهم ما لا يطيقون .
The ruling on learning the principles of religion with summary and detailed evidences. And His saying: (muḥattam) is the predicate, so 'al-ʿilmu al-wāqiʿu' is the subject. (1) Muḥattam is a passive participle, meaning the Lawgiver obligated it and did not permit its neglect. So it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person, male or female, as a personal obligation to know every creed with a proof, even if summary. The evidence for that is His saying: 'So know that there is no god except Allah' [Muhammad: 19]. As for knowing the creeds with detailed evidences, it is a communal obligation. Its meaning is that it is obligatory upon the people of every region, from which reaching other regions is difficult, that among them there be those who know the creeds with detailed proof, because perhaps a doubt may arise and he would repel it. This is the view held by the majority of scholars who made it a personal obligation upon every legally responsible person, male or female, to know every creed with its detailed proof. But this is not satisfactory, for these people have narrowed what Allah, exalted, has made wide for His servants, and have placed in the religion of Allah hardship and extreme difficulty, while Allah is more kind to His servants than to burden them with what they cannot bear.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the ruling of learning the principles of religion. The author begins by stating that the word 'muḥattam' (absolutely obligatory) is the predicate of a nominal sentence where 'al-ʿilmu al-wāqiʿu' (the actual knowledge) is the subject. In the footnote, he explains that 'muḥattam' means the Lawgiver obligated it and did not permit its neglect. Therefore, knowing every creed with a proof, even a summary one, is a personal obligation (farḍ ʿayn) upon every legally responsible person, male or female. The evidence is the verse 'So know that there is no god except Allah.' Then he addresses detailed evidences: knowing creeds with detailed proofs is a communal obligation (farḍ kifāyah), meaning that in every region where access to scholars is difficult, there must be someone capable of detailed proof to repel doubts. The author notes that a group of scholars made detailed knowledge a personal obligation, but he criticizes this view as overly restrictive, because Allah has not burdened people beyond their capacity.

Key Terms
محتم
فرض عين
فرض كفاية
دليل إجمالي
دليل تفصيلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that knowing the creed with detailed proofs is a personal obligation on every Muslim.
Correction: The passage clarifies that detailed proofs are only a communal obligation, not a personal one, because Allah does not burden souls beyond their capacity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on knowing creeds with detailed evidences?
A) Personal obligation (farḍ ʿayn)
B) Communal obligation (farḍ kifāyah)
C) Recommended (mandūb)
D) Permissible (mubāḥ)

Key Takeaway
Every Muslim must know the basics of creed with proof, but detailed proofs are only required from some to guard the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you personally ensured that you know at least one proof for each core creed, or have you been content to follow blindly? What will you do today to learn one proof?
?
The author says detailed proof is a communal obligation. Are you actively part of the community fulfilling this duty, or do you leave it to others? If you are not, what step can you take to support those who do?
?
Reflect on your own pursuit of knowledge: have you ever felt burdened by excessive detail or discouraged by complexity? How can you seek knowledge while trusting that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity?

Method of Learning
beginner
6 min
The Absolute Obligation of Teaching and Learning Tawhid
The commentator states the absolute obligation of tawhid learning and teaching and discusses taqlid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that learning and teaching tawhid is an absolutely obligatory duty in Islam, supported by Qur'anic evidence and acknowledging the ease of the Shari'a for common people.

Core Idea
Teaching and learning tawhid is a non-negotiable duty, but the Shari'a accommodates limitations.

In this passage, the commentator explains the foundational obligation of acquiring and imparting knowledge of tawhid. Building on the matn, he clarifies that this obligation is absolute, with no concession, and then addresses a crucial debate: is simple taqlid (blind acceptance) sufficient for the common person? He weighs the evidence and concludes that the first view—that taqlid is insufficient—is stronger, yet he also emphasizes the Prophet's gentleness.

Guided Path
1
Read the main statement
Begin by reading the opening line that declares the absolute obligation of learning and teaching tawhid. Notice the phrasing 'muḥattam' (absolutely binding).
This is the central thesis.
2
Examine the proof
The commentator cites the verse 'Know that there is no god except Allah'. Reflect on how this verse commands knowledge, not mere belief.
The proof is direct.
3
Explore the taqlid debate
Read the discussion about whether taqlid is sufficient. Note the reference to al-Sanūsī's position and the criticism of those who say it is enough.
Pay attention to the commentator's preferred view.
4
Understand the rationale
The commentator justifies the preferred view by noting that common people may not understand proofs, and the Prophet was not strict. This shows the Shari'a's ease.
Balance between obligation and practicality.

يعني أنَّ تعلُّم التّوحيد وتعليمه واجب شرعاً وجوبًا مُحيَّما، أي لا ترخيص فيه ؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد: ١٩] (١): أنَّ تعلُّم التّوحيد وتعليمه واجب (۲) شرعاً وجوبًا مُحيَّما، أي لا ترخيص فيه (۳) ؛ لقوله تعالى (٤): ﴿فَاعْلَمْ أَنَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا اللَّهُ ﴾ [محمد: ١٩] وقيل: هو كافر، وعليه جرى السنوسي في شرح الكبرى ونصره، وشنّع على من قال: إن التقليد كافٍ ، ويقال : إنه رجع إلى القول بكفاية التقليد، والذي تطمئن إليه النَّفس هو القول الأول من هذه الأقوال ؛ لأنَّ مِن العوام مَن لا يستطيع فهم هذه الأدلة ، ولأنَّ الرَّسول كان يقبل من كلِّ واحدٍ ما قدر عليه. ولم يُعرف . عنه التشدد في حمل الناس على إدراك ما لم يمنحهم الله القدرة على إدراكه ، وسيأتي لهذا الكلام تتمة عند إثارة هذه المسألة في كلام المصنف. محي . (۱) قوله: (يعني) إنما أتى بالعناية إشارة إلى أن المراد بالعلم في المتن نفس الفن المعلوم، والباء بعده للتصوير وذلك ليظهر قوله بعد : ( يحتاج للتبيين ... إلخ) من غير تكلف استخدام ولا غيره، ما سبق الإشارة له فليتأمل والتعليم: من صفات الشيخ والتعلم: من صفات الطالب ، والحاصل أنه إنْ أريدَ مِن أصل (الدين المعنى العَلَمي فلا بد من تحويل العبارة ؛ لأنه لا معنى لوجوب الفن المدون إلا من حيث تعليمه وتعلمه، وإن لم يرد منه المعنى العلمي فلا حاجة للتحويل ، والأولى إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها ، وأن معناها التصديق بعقائد الدين أمر واجب محتم ؛ إذ وجوب التعلم والتعليم إنما هو من باب ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو الواجب، واستشكل بأن العلم بمعنى التصديق من باب الكيف، والكيف ليس فعلا فهو ليس مكلف به إلا من حيث أسبابه من التعلم والتعليم، فحينئذ لا بد من تحويل العبارة مطلقا ؛ سواء أريد المعنى العَلَمي أم لم يرد الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (واجب) لم يقل واجبان تنزيلا للتعليم والتعلم منزلة الشيء الواحد لتلازمهما ، النووي: إن العالم لا يجب عليه أن يطلب الجاهل ليُعلمه ، بل الأمر بالعكس، أي: فليس كالرسول ؛ لأن الأحكام يقررها الرسول على الناس، فليبحثوا بعد عمن يعلمهم، نعم يجب على العالم الإجابة بعد الطلب، وكل هذا ما لم يُشاهِد مُنكَرًا مِن الجاهل فيجب حينئذ المبادرة للتعليم والتغيير حسب الإمكان. الأمير . (۳) قوله: (لا ترخيص فيه تفسير لقوله: (محتما) بحيث أن تاركه يكون عاصيا على المعتمد في المقلد الشنواني . (٤) قوله : ( لقوله تعالى : فاعلم ... الخ) قيل : الدليل قاصر على الوحدانية ، وأجيب: بأنها تتضمن العقائد كما سيأتي ، قلنا: ظاهر في الإلهيات وأما النبوات والسمعيات فإنما تؤخذ من جميع
He means that learning tawḥīd and teaching it is obligatory in the law with an absolutely obligatory obligation, i.e., there is no concession in it, due to His saying: 'So know that there is no god except Allah' [Muhammad: 19]. (1) That learning tawḥīd and teaching it is obligatory (2) in the law with an absolutely obligatory obligation, i.e., no concession in it (3); due to His saying (4): 'So know that there is no god except Allah' [Muhammad: 19]. And it was said: He is a disbeliever, and upon this al-Sanūsī proceeded in his commentary al-Kubrā and supported it, and he severely criticized those who said that taqlīd is sufficient. It is said that he retracted to the view that taqlīd is sufficient. The view that the soul feels at ease with is the first of these opinions, because among the common people are those who cannot understand these proofs, and because the Prophet (ﷺ) accepted from each person what he was capable of. He is not known to have been strict in burdening people to grasp what Allah had not granted them the ability to grasp. There will be a continuation of this discussion when this issue is raised in the author's text. Muḥyī. (1) His saying: (yaʿnī) he brought it with the care to indicate that the intended meaning of 'ʿilm' in the text is the known art itself, and the preposition bā' after it is for depiction, so that his saying later (needs clarification ... etc.) appears without artificial usage or other than that, as previously indicated, so let it be pondered. Teaching is from the attributes of the shaykh and learning is from the attributes of the student. The conclusion is that if the literal meaning of 'dīn' in origin is intended, then the expression must be transformed, because there is no meaning to the obligation of the codified art except in terms of its teaching and learning. If the literal meaning is not intended, then there is no need for transformation. The preferable course is to keep the expression on its outward appearance, and that its meaning is that affirming the creeds of the religion is an absolutely obligatory matter, since the obligation of learning and teaching is of the category of 'what is not completed except by it, so it is obligatory.' It is objected that knowledge in the sense of tasdīq is from the category of quality (kayf), and quality is not an act, so it is not a legal obligation except in terms of its causes, namely learning and teaching. Then there is no alternative but to transform the expression absolutely, whether the literal meaning is intended or not, according to al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: (wājib) he did not say 'two obligatory things' by way of regarding teaching and learning as one thing due to their inseparability. Al-Nawawī: The scholar is not required to seek out the ignorant in order to teach him; rather the matter is the opposite, i.e. not like the Messenger, because the rulings are established by the Messenger upon the people, so they should then search for someone to teach them. Yes, the scholar must respond after being asked, and all this applies as long as he does not witness a disapproved act from the ignorant; then he must hasten to teach and change according to ability. Al-Amīr. (3) His saying: (no concession in it) is an interpretation of his saying (muḥattam), such that its abandoner becomes sinful according to the relied-upon view regarding the muqallid. Al-Shanwānī. (4) His saying: (due to His saying: 'So know...') It is said: The evidence is limited to monotheism, and it was answered: It includes the creeds as will come. We say: It is apparent in the divine matters, but as for prophethood and samʿiyyāt, they are taken from all [Qur'anic verses].
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a multilayered commentary. The main gloss begins with 'yaʿnī' (he means), indicating that the previous text's 'ʿilm' refers to the art of tawḥīd itself. The author explains that learning and teaching tawḥīd is an absolutely obligatory obligation (wājib muḥattam) with no concession, citing the Qur'anic verse 'So know that there is no god except Allah.' The commentary then delves into several points: (1) The word 'yaʿnī' signals that the 'ʿilm' in the original text is the known discipline, not just any knowledge. The bā' (in 'bi-jawharat') is for depiction. (2) The author explains why the text says 'wājib' (singular) instead of 'wājibān' (dual) – because teaching and learning are like one thing, interdependent. Al-Nawawī is cited that a scholar is not required to seek out the ignorant to teach; rather the ignorant must seek the scholar, unless the scholar sees a wrongdoing. (3) 'Lā tarkhīṣ fīhi' interprets 'muḥattam', meaning that abandoning it is sinful. (4) A discussion about the evidence from the verse: some say it is specific to monotheism, but it is answered that it includes all creeds. The passage ends with a note that the verse is clear in divine matters, but prophethood and samʿiyyāt are taken from other verses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taqlīd (blind acceptance) is always sufficient for tawḥīd.
Correction: The commentator argues that taqlīd is not sufficient; one must have certain knowledge, but he also acknowledges that the Shari'a does not overly burden the incapable.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the ruling on learning and teaching tawḥīd?
A) It is recommended but not obligatory
B) It is absolutely obligatory with no concession
C) It is only required for scholars
D) It is optional for common people

Key Takeaway
Learning and teaching tawḥīd is an absolute duty, but the Shari'a allows for the limited understanding of common people, so taqlīd is not a default excuse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you truly studied the meaning of 'La ilaha illa Allah' beyond reciting it? Are you satisfied with merely accepting it from your parents or teachers, or have you made the effort to know its implications for your own life?
?
The commentator notes that the Prophet accepted from each person what he could manage. Does your understanding of tawhid match your capacity? Have you ever asked yourself if you are using your God-given intelligence to seek deeper knowledge, or are you content with the minimum?
?
There is a view that those who blindly follow in tawhid are disbelievers. Even if we don't take the harshest opinion, this lesson demands we take learning seriously. How much time this week will you dedicate to studying the essentials of your faith? Will you commit to even 10 minutes daily?

Linguistic and Conceptual Analysis of the Obligation
The commentator analyzes the wording and implications of the obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical and conceptual nuances behind the obligation of learning and teaching tawḥīd, including the meaning of 'ilm, why the singular 'wājib' is used, and the implications of 'muḥattam'.

Core Idea
The precise wording in the matn is purposefully chosen to convey a foundational duty.

In this supplementary commentary, the teacher delves into the technical details of the original matn. He explains why the word 'ilm (knowledge) in the matn refers to the entire discipline of tawḥīd, why the dual form was not used for teaching and learning, and what 'muḥattam' implies legally. This lesson unpacks the scholarly precision behind the simple statement.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'yaʿnī'
The commentator explains that 'yaʿnī' (he means) signals that 'ilm in the matn is the art of tawḥīd itself, not just any knowledge.
The preposition bā' is for depiction.
2
Analyze the singular 'wājib'
Instead of saying 'two obligatory things', the text says 'wājib' (singular) to treat teaching and learning as one inseparable duty.
Reference to al-Nawawī's view.
3
Examine 'muḥattam' and its implications
The term 'muḥattam' (absolutely binding) means no concession; abandoning it is sinful.
This is the relied-upon view.
4
Consider the evidence from the verse
Some argue the verse only commands monotheism, but it is replied that it includes all creeds.
The commentator notes that the verse is clear in divine matters, while prophethood and samʿiyyāt are taken from all verses.

This passage is a multilayered commentary. The main gloss begins with 'yaʿnī' (he means), indicating that the previous text's 'ʿilm' refers to the art of tawḥīd itself. The author explains that learning and teaching tawḥīd is an absolutely obligatory obligation (wājib muḥattam) with no concession, citing the Qur'anic verse 'So know that there is no god except Allah.' The commentary then delves into several points: (1) The word 'yaʿnī' signals that the 'ʿilm' in the original text is the known discipline, not just any knowledge. The bā' (in 'bi-jawharat') is for depiction. (2) The author explains why the text says 'wājib' (singular) instead of 'wājibān' (dual) – because teaching and learning are like one thing, interdependent. Al-Nawawī is cited that a scholar is not required to seek out the ignorant to teach; rather the ignorant must seek the scholar, unless the scholar sees a wrongdoing. (3) 'Lā tarkhīṣ fīhi' interprets 'muḥattam', meaning that abandoning it is sinful. (4) A discussion about the evidence from the verse: some say it is specific to monotheism, but it is answered that it includes all creeds. The passage ends with a note that the verse is clear in divine matters, but prophethood and samʿiyyāt are taken from other verses.

Key Terms
علم
التصديق
تقليد
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'wājib' in the matn refers to two separate obligations.
Correction: The commentator explains that it is singular to indicate the inseparability of teaching and learning.

Checkpoint
Why does the text use 'wājib' (singular) instead of 'wājibān' (dual)?
A) Grammatical error
B) Because teaching and learning are considered one inseparable duty
C) Because only teaching is obligatory
D) Because only learning is obligatory

Key Takeaway
The precise wording in the matn is carefully chosen: 'yaʿnī' directs to the discipline, singular 'wājib' unifies the duty, and 'muḥattam' underscores its binding nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the obligation of learning and teaching is 'what is not completed except by it, so it is obligatory.' What step have you taken this week to learn or to teach something about the creeds of Islam? If none, what is holding you back?
?
The text says the scholar is not required to seek out the ignorant, but must respond when asked. Are you actively seeking knowledge from scholars? Or are you waiting to be asked? How can you change that?
?
The 'no concession' (muḥattam) means its abandoner becomes sinful. Do you treat learning 'aqeedah as a matter of life and death, or as an optional topic? What would your life look like if you took that burden seriously?

Understanding 'يعني' in the Poem: Knowledge as the Discipline
The commentator explains the function of 'يعني' and the meaning of 'العلم' as the discipline.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator interprets 'يعني' as indicating that 'العلم' refers to the discipline of kalam, and the distinction between teaching and learning.

Core Idea
The poet's 'يعني' tells us that knowledge here means the discipline, not just any belief.

The commentary on the poem begins by unpacking the word 'يعني' – a small word with a big implication. The author uses it to signal that when the poem says 'knowledge', it means the entire discipline of kalam, not just the act of believing. This understanding is crucial for the subsequent argument about obligation. Let's see how the commentator explains this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the function of 'يعني'
Notice that the commentator says the poet used 'يعني' to indicate that 'knowledge' refers to the discipline itself (الفن المعلوم). Also note the function of the 'باء' for illustration.
The 'باء' is for picturing, as in 'by' evidence.
2
Distinguish teaching and learning
The commentator then notes that 'teaching' is an attribute of the shaykh and 'learning' is an attribute of the student. This sets up the later discussion about obligation.
3
Reflect on why this matters
Ask yourself: why does the commentator go into such detail about a single word? Because it affects the entire meaning of the poem regarding the obligation of knowledge.
This connects to the larger debate about converting the expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (يعني) إنما أتى بالعناية إشارة إلى أن المراد بالعلم في المتن نفس الفن المعلوم، والباء بعده للتصوير وذلك ليظهر قوله بعد : ( يحتاج للتبيين ... إلخ) من غير تكلف استخدام ولا غيره، ما سبق الإشارة له فليتأمل والتعليم: من صفات الشيخ والتعلم: من صفات الطالب ، والحاصل أنه إنْ أريدَ مِن أصل (الدين المعنى العَلَمي فلا بد من تحويل العبارة ؛ لأنه لا معنى لوجوب الفن المدون إلا من حيث تعليمه وتعلمه، وإن لم يرد منه المعنى العلمي فلا حاجة للتحويل ، والأولى إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها ، وأن معناها التصديق بعقائد الدين أمر واجب محتم ؛ إذ وجوب التعلم والتعليم إنما هو من باب ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو الواجب، واستشكل بأن العلم بمعنى التصديق من باب الكيف، والكيف ليس فعلا فهو ليس مكلف به إلا من حيث أسبابه من التعلم والتعليم، فحينئذ لا بد من تحويل العبارة مطلقا ؛ سواء أريد المعنى العَلَمي أم لم يرد الشنواني.
His saying: (meaning) he only came with the use of 'يعني' as an indication that what is meant by knowledge in the text is the discipline itself, and the ba' after it is for picturing, in order that his later saying: (it needs clarification... etc.) appears without affectation or other issues, as previously indicated, so let it be contemplated. And teaching: from the attributes of the shaykh, and learning: from the attributes of the student. The conclusion is that if the meaning of 'usul al-din' is intended as the technical sense, then the expression must be converted, because there is no meaning to the obligation of the compiled discipline except from the perspective of teaching and learning it. And if the technical sense is not intended, there is no need for conversion. The preferred approach is to keep the expression on its apparent meaning, and that its meaning is that believing in the doctrines of the religion is an absolute obligation, since the obligation of learning and teaching is of the category of that without which the obligatory cannot be completed, so it is obligatory. And it is objected that knowledge in the sense of belief is of the category of quality, and quality is not an act, so one is not obligated to it except from the perspective of its causes, which are learning and teaching. Then there is no avoiding the conversion of the expression absolutely, whether the technical meaning is intended or not. Al-Shinwani.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the word 'يعني' used in the poem. The commentator explains that the author's use of 'يعني' indicates that the term 'العلم' in the text refers to the discipline itself (الفن المعلوم). The ba' in 'بالأدلة' is for illustration. This is to make the subsequent phrase 'يحتاج للتبيين' appear naturally. Then the commentator distinguishes between teaching (from the shaykh) and learning (from the student). The main point is the need to convert the expression: if the intended meaning of 'أصول الدين' is the technical sense (the compiled discipline), then the obligation applies to teaching and learning it, not to the discipline itself. If the technical sense is not intended, no conversion is needed. The preferred view is to keep the apparent meaning, which is that belief in the doctrines is obligatory. However, an objection is raised that belief is a quality, not an act, so one is only obligated through its causes (learning and teaching). Therefore, conversion is absolutely necessary. The commentator attributes this to al-Shinwani.

Key Terms
يعني
الفن المعلوم
التعليم
التعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People might think 'العلم' in the poem means any kind of knowledge.
Correction: The commentator explains it specifically means the discipline of kalam.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say the poet indicates by using 'يعني'?
A) That knowledge is obligatory.
B) That knowledge refers to the discipline itself.
C) That teaching is required.
D) That learning is necessary.

Key Takeaway
The poet's 'يعني' is a precise indicator that 'العلم' in this context refers to the science of kalam itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a small word like 'يعني' in a scholarly text, do you pause to consider its precise function, or do you rush past it? Think of one specific instance where missing such an indicator might have led you to misunderstand a verse or hadith. What will you change today to ensure you give each word its due weight?
?
The commentator says 'التعليم من صفات الشيخ والتعلم من صفات الطالب.' In your current pursuit of knowledge, which attribute do you most consistently embody? Where have you fallen short, and what concrete step can you take this week to strengthen your role as a true student?
?
The commentator invites us to contemplate: 'فليتأمل' — so let it be contemplated. When was the last time you truly contemplated a line of kalam, not just reading but wrestling with its implications? Commit to spending 10 minutes today in silent reflection on one key sentence from a book of aqeedah. What sentence will you choose?

Resolving the Expression: Converting the Phrase for Obligation
The commentator discusses the need to convert the expression and al-Shinwani's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate about converting the poem's expression and why some scholars argue it is absolutely necessary.

Core Idea
The obligation to believe is not direct; it requires learning and teaching, forcing a conversion of the text.

After clarifying the meaning of 'knowledge', the commentator tackles a crucial issue: does the poem's statement that 'knowledge is obligatory' need to be converted? The conclusion depends on whether 'knowledge' means the discipline or the act of belief. An objection from al-Shinwani adds a twist: belief is a quality, not an act, so obligation must be through its causes. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two scenarios
The commentator says if the intended meaning is the technical sense (the discipline), then obligation applies to teaching/learning it. If not, no conversion needed.
The technical sense is 'الفن المعلوم'.
2
The preferred view
Despite the above, the commentator prefers to keep the apparent meaning: belief in doctrines is obligatory, because learning/teaching are necessary means.
This is based on the principle: 'ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب'.
3
The objection by al-Shinwani
Al-Shinwani objects that belief is a quality, and we are only obligated through its causes (learning and teaching). Therefore, conversion is absolutely necessary.
Quality (الكيف) is not an act; commands address acts.
4
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on the depth of this discussion: even a single line of poetry requires careful reasoning about obligation and the nature of belief.
This shows the precision of kalam methodology.

Source Text

قوله: (يعني) إنما أتى بالعناية إشارة إلى أن المراد بالعلم في المتن نفس الفن المعلوم، والباء بعده للتصوير وذلك ليظهر قوله بعد : ( يحتاج للتبيين ... إلخ) من غير تكلف استخدام ولا غيره، ما سبق الإشارة له فليتأمل والتعليم: من صفات الشيخ والتعلم: من صفات الطالب ، والحاصل أنه إنْ أريدَ مِن أصل (الدين المعنى العَلَمي فلا بد من تحويل العبارة ؛ لأنه لا معنى لوجوب الفن المدون إلا من حيث تعليمه وتعلمه، وإن لم يرد منه المعنى العلمي فلا حاجة للتحويل ، والأولى إبقاء العبارة على ظاهرها ، وأن معناها التصديق بعقائد الدين أمر واجب محتم ؛ إذ وجوب التعلم والتعليم إنما هو من باب ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو الواجب، واستشكل بأن العلم بمعنى التصديق من باب الكيف، والكيف ليس فعلا فهو ليس مكلف به إلا من حيث أسبابه من التعلم والتعليم، فحينئذ لا بد من تحويل العبارة مطلقا ؛ سواء أريد المعنى العَلَمي أم لم يرد الشنواني.
